<p><center><h2>Birds of Japan:
<p>Identification, Distribution and Conservation</h2></center>
<!--LINE1-->
<br>
<center><img src="Photos/SLWarbler.jpg" width="250" height="120" alt="Sakhalin Leaf Warbler">
<p><h6>Photo courtesy of <a href="http://www.avesphoto.com/website/JP/home.htm"> Mike Danzenbaker</a>. No unauthorized use</h6>
</center>

<br><br><br>



<p><i>Yoku irasshaimashitta</i> and Welcome.
<p>This website is a cooperative effort by the members of the Yahoo Japan English-Language Birders Group, <b>Kantori</b>.  It is an accumulation and collocation of information from our membership group postings and other sources on the Japanese avifauna of interest to the membership.  Although the website language is English, we would particularly like to thank our Japanese friends and colleagues, without whose knowledge and contributions this site would be considerably more barren.
<p>This is a not-for-profit pastiche site, and has gleaned information from many sources, all of which we wish to acknowledge. If any photograph, link or text item is uncredited or improperly credited, please advise the <a href="mailto: MisterMicawber@gmail.com">site administrator</a>.  Text may have been edited for spelling, grammar, clarity and brevity:  contributors, please complain if you feel that you have been ill-served by this process.

<p>To become a member of the Kantori listserve (about "birds and
birdwatching in Japan") go to <a href="http://groups.yahoo.com/group/kantori/">Kantori Group</a>
or...
<p><center> <a href="http://groups.yahoo.com/group/kantori/join">
  <img src="http://us.i1.yimg.com/us.yimg.com/i/yg/img/i/us/ui/join.gif"
       style="border: 0px;"
       alt="Click to join kantori"/>
  <p>Click to join kantori</p>
</a></center>

<div class='blog-title'>
<link href="http://coolbirds.net/posts.rss?title=CoolBirds.net+Blog" rel="alternate" title="RSS" type="application/rss+xml" />
<a href="/posts.rss"><img alt="Feed" src="http://coolbirds.net/images/icons/feed.png?1272666365" /></a> Blog
</div>
<div class='blog-title'>
User lists, bird lists, graphic design & reputation engine
</div>
<div class='blog-date'>
28  March 2010 - about 1 month ago
</div>
<div class='blog-post'>
<p>Hey all birders.</p>
<p>Please note the Users button on the menu bar at the top of the page.&nbsp; This will show you all active birders on CoolBirds.net, from there you can click on the name of the birder and see their year lists.&nbsp; This is now open to all, you don't have to be logged in to see bird lists.</p>
<p>Also note that I've redone the CoolBirds.net logo, its now in a cooler font.&nbsp; The last version was a bit boring, I must admit.</p>
<p>I'm busy working on some more home page design issues and rethinking the scoring system.&nbsp;&nbsp; Currently reputation is based on a simple calculation reputation = sightings + (votes * 2) + (unique birds * 2).&nbsp; This design has many limitations, it's dynamically calculated meaning any rule change affects historical reputation and its difficult to sort users by reputation using Ruby on Rails' Active Record finders.&nbsp; I've decided to prototype an event based system that will run a list of rules for every type of activity e.g. Log a sighting, comment on a sighting, add a photo etc.&nbsp; These will result in the the outcome of the rule being saved separately in a reputation table which is associated with the user, solving the sorting issue.&nbsp; Also, this way its easy to see how and why you earned reputation and historical reputation levels won't be affected by rule changes, which are inevitable. Rule changes will be apply from deployment time onward, whenever their specific type of activity happens.</p>
<p>This is all designed to reward birders for birding, and encourage users to get the most complete experience out of the site possible.</p>
<p>Happy birding =)</p>

</div>

function MM_preloadImages() { //v3.0
  var d=document; if(d.images){ if(!d.MM_p) d.MM_p=new Array();
    var i,j=d.MM_p.length,a=MM_preloadImages.arguments; for(i=0; i<a.length; i++)
    if (a[i].indexOf("#")!=0){ d.MM_p[j]=new Image; d.MM_p[j++].src=a[i];}}
}

function MM_findObj(n, d) { //v4.01
  var p,i,x;  if(!d) d=document; if((p=n.indexOf("?"))>0&&parent.frames.length) {d=parent.frames[n.substring(p+1)].document; n=n.substring(0,p);}
  if(!(x=d[n])&&d.all) x=d.all[n]; for (i=0;!x&&i<d.forms.length;i++) x=d.forms[i][n];
  for(i=0;!x&&d.layers&&i<d.layers.length;i++) x=MM_findObj(n,d.layers[i].document);
  for(i=0;!x&&d.layers&&i<d.layers.length;i++) x=MM_findObj(n,d.layers[i].document);
  if(!x && d.getElementById) x=d.getElementById(n); return x;
}

function MM_nbGroup(event, grpName) { //v6.0
  var i,img,nbArr,args=MM_nbGroup.arguments;
  if (event == "init" && args.length > 2) {
    if ((img = MM_findObj(args[2])) != null && !img.MM_init) {
      img.MM_init = true; img.MM_up = args[3]; img.MM_dn = img.src;
      if ((nbArr = document[grpName]) == null) nbArr = document[grpName] = new Array();
      nbArr[nbArr.length] = img;
      for (i=4; i < args.length-1; i+=2) if ((img = MM_findObj(args[i])) != null) {
        if (!img.MM_up) img.MM_up = img.src;
        img.src = img.MM_dn = args[i+1];
        nbArr[nbArr.length] = img;
      }}
    } else if (event == "over") {
      document.MM_nbOver = nbArr = new Array();
      for (i=1; i < args.length-1; i+=3) if ((img = MM_findObj(args[i])) != null) {
        if (!img.MM_up) img.MM_up = img.src;
        img.src = (img.MM_dn && args[i+2]) ? args[i+2] : ((args[i+1])? args[i+1] : img.MM_up);
        nbArr[nbArr.length] = img;
      }
    } else if (event == "out" ) {
      for (i=0; i < document.MM_nbOver.length; i++) {
        img = document.MM_nbOver[i]; img.src = (img.MM_dn) ? img.MM_dn : img.MM_up; }
    } else if (event == "down") {
      nbArr = document[grpName];
      if (nbArr)
        for (i=0; i < nbArr.length; i++) { img=nbArr[i]; img.src = img.MM_up; img.MM_dn = 0; }
      document[grpName] = nbArr = new Array();
      for (i=2; i < args.length-1; i+=2) if ((img = MM_findObj(args[i])) != null) {
        if (!img.MM_up) img.MM_up = img.src;
        img.src = img.MM_dn = (args[i+1])? args[i+1] : img.MM_up;
        nbArr[nbArr.length] = img;
    }}
}

function popup(mylink, windowname) {
  if (! window.focus)return true;
  var href;
  if (typeof(mylink) == 'string')
    href=mylink;
  else
    href=mylink.href;
  window.open(href, windowname, 'left=300,top=300,width=400,height=200,scrollbars=no,dependent=yes');
return false;
}

  <td width="680" valign="top"><table  border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" id="Content">
    <tr><td><table border="0" width="680" align="center" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="7" id="Narrative">
      <tr><td align="center">
        <h1><a name="HDR"></a>Birding Tours to the American Tropics<br>Bird & Wildlife Nature Photo Tours</h1>
        <h2>Central & South America Specialists<br>Belize, Brazil, Costa Rica, Ecuador, Guyana, Panama, Peru</h2>
      </td></tr>
      <tr><td><h3 align="center"><b>SMALL GROUP TOURS WITH MAXIMUM 8 PARTICIPANTS</b></h3></td></tr>
      <tr><td align="justify">
        <div id="tabmenu1">
          <ul>
            <li id="selected"><a href="#">About Our Tours</a></li>
            <li><a href="birding-tours.html">Birding Tours</a></li>
            <li><a href="photo-tours.html">Photo Tours</a></li>
            <li><a href="news.html"><font color="#FF0000">What's New</font></a></li>
            <li><a href="registration.html">How To Register</a></li>
            <li><a href="guarantee.html">Guarantee</a></li>
          </ul>
        </div>
        <div id="tabcontent">
          <p align="justify" class="MediumNormal"><img src="images/blond-crested-woodpecker-001-LF-rt.jpg" alt="Blond-crested Woodpecker - &copy; Laura L Fellows and Exotic Birding Tours" hspace="8" align="right"><b>Exotic Birding offers two types of tours to Central and South America, <a href="#BT" class="LinkStyle">birding tours</a> and <a href="#PT" class="LinkStyle">bird/wildlife photo tours</a></b>. Our goal is always to provide a quality and enjoyable travel experience as well as a successful tour in terms of species seen or photographs obtained. We are sometimes asked if the countries we visit are safe for travel. Exotic Birding carefully considers safety of all tour destinations and does not offer tours to places we consider unsafe for travel.</p>
          <p align="justify" class="MediumNormal">Every birding tour and every bird photo tour is led by a highly qualified birding guide and an accomplished bird photographer, but the two types of tours differ in emphasis. Our birding tours are for devoted birders of any skill level seeking to maximize species count. Our bird/wildlife photo tours are conducted similarly to our birding tours but are slower paced, emphasize photography instead of species count, and are intended for bird/wildlife photography enthusiasts. The goal of our photo tours is to photograph many different birds and other animals, not just a single species or group of birds.</p>
          <p align="justify" class="MediumNormal">We're happy to provide participants of each tour with a CD of photos taken by our tour leader, Laura Fellows, upon request. We encourage you to visit our galleries of full-size images taken by Laura on prior tours.</p>
          <p align="justify" class="MediumNormal">We recommend that tour participants arrive a day early for most tours in case of flight/weather delays. We'll be happy to reserve any extra nights you need at the same rate you would pay if you make the reservations yourself.</p>
          <p align="justify" class="MediumNormal">Feel free to call us any time. We provide personalized service and won't subject you to a phone menu or sales pitch when you call. <b>Interested persons should register as early as possible to ensure a place on the tour. Deposits are refundable until 90 days prior to tour departure.</b> To register print the registration form accessed by clicking the sidebar link and mail it to us with your deposit.</p>
          <p align="justify" class="LargeBold"><a name="BT">EXOTIC BIRDING TOURS</a></p>
          <p align="justify" class="MediumNormal"><img src="images/rufous-capped-thornbill-001-LF-lf.jpg" alt="Rufous-capped Thornbill - &copy; Laura L Fellows and Exotic Birding Tours" hspace="8" align="left"><b>We've carefully planned and organized every birding tour to maximize our birding success by visiting as many habitats, altitudinal zones, and locales as possible while still maintaining a reasonable tour pace</b>. Each tour is led by an English-speaking master birding guide who usually lives and guides in the destination country year-round. Accommodations are generally comfortable eco-lodges or hotels situated near birding locales unless otherwise noted on our tour pages. Our guides are well versed at finding and identifying rarities and target birds, always with the overall goal of making our birding experience as successful as possible.</p>
          <p align="justify" class="MediumNormal">We offer <a href="costarica-birding-tours.html" class="LinkStyle" title="Costa Rica tour schedule">Costa Rica</a>, <a href="ecuador-birding-tours.html" class="LinkStyle" title="Ecuador tour schedule">Ecuador</a>, and <a href="panama-birding-tours.html" class="LinkStyle" title="Panama tour schedule">Panama</a> birding tours every year because these are our most popular destinations. We also offer a birding tour to <a href="belize-birding-tours.html" class="LinkStyle" title="Belize tour schedule">Belize</a> most years. We began offering a birding tour to <a href="guyana-birding-tours.html" class="LinkStyle" title="Guyana tour schedule">Guyana</a> in 2010 and will begin offering a birding tour to <a href="brazil-birding-tours.html" class="LinkStyle" title="Brazil tour schedule">Brazil</a> in 2011. We plan on offering future tours to Colombia, <a href="peru-birding-tours.html" class="LinkStyle" title="Peru tour schedule">Peru</a>, and the <a href="galapagos-islands.html" class="LinkStyle" title="About the Galapagos Islands">Galapagos Islands</a> as well. <a href="#TOP" class="BackStyle">TOP</a></p>
          <p align="justify" class="LargeBold"><a name="PT">EXOTIC BIRD/WILDLIFE NATURE PHOTO TOURS</a></p>
          <p align="justify" class="MediumNormal"><img src="images/toco-toucan-024-LF-rt.jpg" alt="Toco Toucan - &copy; Laura L Fellows and Exotic Birding Tours" hspace="8" align="right"><b>Our bird/wildlife photo tours are designed to capture images of a broad diversity of tropical birds. We photograph hummingbirds visiting feeders, hawks and eagles in open habitats, and a wide variety of toucans, antbirds, tanagers, and other birds within rainforests and other habitats</b>. While we emphasize bird photography, we also photograph caimans, tamanduas, monkeys, and other wildlife as opportunities arise.</p>
          <p align="justify" class="MediumNormal">In the past photographers wishing to photograph a broad spectrum of birds had to strike out on their own or join a birding tour where their photography often conflicts with the goals of serious birders. For this reason we've created birding-like tours that emphasize photography rather than species count but are still designed to seek out many different kinds of birds. To our knowledge we are the only tour company currently offering bird photo tours jointly led by a professional bird photographer and an expert birding guide.</p>
          <p align="justify" class="MediumNormal"><img src="images/montezuma-oropendola-004-LF-lf.jpg" hspace="8" align="left">Every photo tour is organized and led by Laura Fellows, who created the images on our website. Every tour is co-led by an expert birding guide whose role is to find and identify subjects for our photography in even the most challenging tropical environments. Our tours are not workshops, but Laura is happy to assist participants with their techniques upon request.</p>
          <p align="justify" class="MediumNormal">We offer bird/wildlife photo tours to <a href="belize-photo-tours.html" class="LinkStyle" title="Belize tour schedule">Belize</a>, <a href="costarica-photo-tours.html" class="LinkStyle" title="Costa Rica tour schedule">Costa Rica</a>, <a href="ecuador-photo-tours.html" class="LinkStyle" title="Ecuador tour schedule">Ecuador</a>, and <a href="panama-photo-tours.html" class="LinkStyle" title="Panama tour schedule">Panama</a> most years. We plan on offering a photo tour to the <a href="galapagos-islands.html" class="LinkStyle" title="About the Galapagos Islands">Galapagos Islands</a> in future. For more information about our upcoming tours, please contact us by phone or email. <a href="#TOP" class="BackStyle">TOP</a></p>
          <p align="justify" class="MediumNormal">&nbsp;</p>
          <p align="justify" class="MediumNormal">&nbsp;</p>
        </div>
      </td></tr>

    function setAttributeOnload(object, attribute, val) {
      if(window.addEventListener) {
        window.addEventListener("load",
          function(){ object[attribute] = val; }, false);
      } else {
        window.attachEvent('onload', function(){ object[attribute] = val; });

<h2 class='date-header'><span>Saturday, April 10, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='1803159827239890547'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://highlatitudebirdwatcher.blogspot.com/2010/04/ruddy-long-way-to-fly.html'>A Ruddy Long Way to Fly</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
<i></i><br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><i><a href="http://www.blogger.com/post-edit.g?blogID=1796788122402177466&amp;postID=1803159827239890547" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="123" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S7-X8rhqJ3I/AAAAAAAAAmI/pdzDmiB07gg/s400/bl_ruddy.jpg" width="400" /> </a></i></div><i><span lang="EN">"Ruddy Turnstone is a small, highly-migratory wading  bird with a large global range. It breeds in northern latitudes in open  tundra habitat often close to water. Outside the breeding season it is  found along coastlines, particularly on rocky or stony shores. It is the  only species of turnstone in much of its range and is often called  Turnstone.</span></i><br />
<br />
<i><span lang="EN">"We have been amazed at the feats of Bar-tailed Godwit tracked by  satellite from Australia and New Zealand to their breeding grounds in  the high Arctic and back", said Dr Clive Minton from the Australasian  Wader Studies Group. "Unfortunately the size of the satellite  transmitters, and the batteries required to power them, precluded their  use on smaller shorebirds like Ruddy Turnstone".</span></i><br />
<br />
<div class="pullquote"><i>&#8220;&#8230; it was back in south-east Australia having  completed a <span style="font-weight: bold;">27,000</span>-km round trip&#8221; <span class="quotename">&#8212;Ken Gosbell</span><span class="quotetitle">, Chairman of the Australasian Wader Studies Group </span></i> </div><br />
<i>The researchers therefore decided to use new 1 gram light-sensor  geolocators - supplied by British Antarctic Survey in Cambridge, England  - and fitted them to eight Ruddy Turnstone spending their non-breeding  season in south-east Australia in April 2009. Four geolocators were  eventually retrieved from birds between 20 October 2009 and 8 January  2010.</i><br />
<br />
<i>"All four birds flew 7,600 km non-stop to Taiwan in just over six  days, with three apparently travelling together", said Dr Clive Minton.  They then flew on to northern Siberia, following separate paths and  stopping over at different sites. "By early August, two had moved  to Korea and south-eastern Siberia, respectively, but another bird  returned to Australia via the Central Pacific!"</i><br />
<br />
<i>The Pacific bird spent 26 July -15 October on the Aleutian Islands  before flying 6,200 km across the Pacific in four days to Kiribati, and  then it made another four-day, 5,000-km flight to eastern Australia.  "Five days later it was back in south-east Australia having completed a  27,000-km round trip", added Ken Gosbell - Chairman of the Australasian  Wader Studies Group."</i><br />
<i><br />
</i><br />
Read the whole story at: <a href="http://www.birdlife.org/news/news/2010/04/ruddy-turnstone-migration.html">A Ruddy Long Way to Fly</a><span id="goog_1061544393"></span><span id="goog_1061544394"></span><br />
<br />
I just wonder how long flights our Ruddy Turnstones make...?
<div style='clear: both;'></div>
</div>

<h2 class='date-header'><span>Wednesday, April 7, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='874380900150868495'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://highlatitudebirdwatcher.blogspot.com/2010/04/satellite-osprey-jukka-is-on-road-to.html'>Satellite Osprey Jukka is on the road to home!</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
Jukka is coming back, at the moment he is in Croatia. In summer 2009 the project, started in 2007  in collaboration between the  Finnish Museum of Natural History, <a href="http://www.saaksisaatio.fi/en/index.htm">the Osprey  Foundation</a>  and <a href="http://www.upm-kymmene.com/en/">UPM</a>, continued  monitoring the life of a male Osprey with the  help of the newest  generation of GPS-Argos satellite transmitters.<br />
<br />
See the whole story at: <a href="http://www.fmnh.helsinki.fi/english/zoology/satelliteospreys/jukka/index.htm">Satellite  Osprey Jukka - Zoological Museum - FMNH</a><br />
<a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S7ySUwIf7QI/AAAAAAAAAmA/FMUVUTcET5k/s1600/600_jukkasaaksi3_hannu_vainiopekka.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"></a><a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S7ySUwIf7QI/AAAAAAAAAmA/FMUVUTcET5k/s1600/600_jukkasaaksi3_hannu_vainiopekka.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="267" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S7ySUwIf7QI/AAAAAAAAAmA/FMUVUTcET5k/s400/600_jukkasaaksi3_hannu_vainiopekka.jpg" width="400" /></a><br />

<h2 class='date-header'><span>Monday, April 5, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='7610250089930093921'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://highlatitudebirdwatcher.blogspot.com/2010/04/birding-in-fog-and-rain-by-mountain.html'>Birding in fog and rain by mountain bike</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S7ozvARXm_I/AAAAAAAAAl4/Z4zx8WzkW2o/s1600/sumu.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="300" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S7ozvARXm_I/AAAAAAAAAl4/Z4zx8WzkW2o/s400/sumu.jpg" width="400" /></a></div>I don't really know which one is easier; birding in rain or birding in fog. During last days I have done both.<br />
They both have downsides and advantages. In fog, the visibility is really poor and the tiny water particles cover all surfaces. But the birds are active and you can still find them relatively easy. In light rain, the visibility is usually better, but the on the other hand, the birds don't move a lot and all your gears and clothing get wet fast.<br />
<br />
Still, with proper clothing and gears, birding in in fog or rain is not too difficult - on the contrary, it can be really enjoyable. Migratory birds that usually just fly over your area, may drop down and stay sedentary until weather gets better for migration. Many rarities have been also found during these "bad" weather conditions.<br />
<br />
As our car broke down on Saturday, I have used my mountain bike for transportation while birding. It was cool to use bike for birding after long winter, and I will continue to use it also after the car is fixed, even though it diminishes the area that I can check during limited birding hours. While cycling, you will see and especially hear much more birds than while driving by car.<br />
<br />
So, what I saw during these days? Lot's of European Robins <i>(Erithacus rubecula)</i>, some Mistle Thrushes <i>(Turdus viscivorus)</i> and Song Thrushes <i>(Turdus philomela)</i>, flocks of 50 and 30 Fieldfares <i>(Turdus pilaris)</i>, 20 Bohemian Waxwings <i>(Bombycilla garrulus)</i>, Eurasian Coot <i>(Fulica atra)</i>, Grey Heron <i>(Ardea cinerea)</i>, Common Cranes <i>(Grus grus)</i>, Whooper Swans <i>(Cygnus cygnus)</i>, Canada Goose <i>(Branta canadensis)</i>, Kestrel<i> (Falco tinnunculus)</i>, hundreds of Chaffinches <i>(Fringilla coelebes)</i>, Black-headed Gulls<i> (Chroicocephalus ridibundus)</i>, Northern Lapwings <i>(Vanellus vanellus)</i>, European Goldfinches <i>(Carduelis carduelis)</i> and many more.<br />
<br />
Easter holiday is now over, back to work tomorrow... I hope the car will get fixed soon, as I'd love to make birding trips flood areas of Akaa and KylmÃ¤koski when the migration of Taiga Bean Geese start - and that will be soon!

<h2 class='date-header'><span>Saturday, April 3, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='8381912298403478594'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://highlatitudebirdwatcher.blogspot.com/2010/04/spring-is-here.html'>The Spring is here</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S7Zj49sfFvI/AAAAAAAAAlg/FIoxTYtrRCA/s1600/joutsen.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="296" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S7Zj49sfFvI/AAAAAAAAAlg/FIoxTYtrRCA/s400/joutsen.jpg" width="400" /></a></div>&nbsp;The first spring month, March, was exceptionally cold this year, it felt like a winter. Very few birds arrived during March; mainly some Goosanders <i>(Mergus merganser)</i>, Common Goldeneyes <i>(Bucephala clangula)</i> and Herring Gulls <i>(Larus argentatus)</i>. But finally in the end of March, the weather changed and the spring rolled in to the north. <br />
<br />
The rush of birds was great it continues still. Lots of species have arrived during last few days and they are rather easy to find as they concentrate in few places where the snow has already melted away. Skylarks <i>(Alauda arvensis)</i>, Chaffinches <i>(Fringilla coelebs)</i>, Bramblings <i>(Fringilla montifringilla)</i>, Common Starlings <i>(Sturnus vulgaris)</i>, Reed Buntings <i>(Emberiza schoeniclus)</i>, European Robins <i>(Erithacus rubecula)</i>, Meadow Pipits <i>(Anthus pratensis)</i> and many other passerines can be found in the fields. Bigger birds have arrived as well; Northern Lapwings <i>(Vanellus vanellus)</i>, Wood Pigeons <i>(Columba palumbus)</i>, Kestrels<i> (Falco tinnunculus)</i>, Common Buzzards <i>(Buteo buteo)</i>, Rough-legged Buzzards <i>(Buteo lagopus)</i>, Common Cranes <i>(Grus grus)</i>, Grey Heron <i>(Ardea cinerea)</i>, Whooper Swans <i>(Cygnus cygnus)</i>, Grey-lag Goose <i>(Anser anser)</i> and Canada Goose<i> (Branta canadensis)</i> can all be found now. And from now on, new species will arrive every day.<br />
<br />
<a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S7ZkbcWsrvI/AAAAAAAAAlo/GiEh8ix2U9A/s1600/hyypp%C3%A41.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="277" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S7ZkbcWsrvI/AAAAAAAAAlo/GiEh8ix2U9A/s400/hyypp%C3%A41.jpg" width="400" /></a>During last days I have found with my younger son Roni also some interesting species; an adult Western Lesser Black-backed Gull <i>(Larus fuscus graellsii / intermedius)</i> was in&nbsp; Viiala in the evening of 31st of March and today we found a Woodlark <i>(Lullula arborea)</i> from MetsÃ¤kansa. The Western Lesser Black-backed Gulls are rare in Finland and Woodlark is uncommon in this part of Pirkanmaa county. Oh, and few weeks ago we saw also 4 White-tailed Eagles <i>(Haliaeetus albicilla)</i> in LempÃ¤Ã¤lÃ¤.<br />
<br />
Snow is melting now very fast, daytime temperature has been +10 degrees Celcius and next two days we should get lots of rain... that means flooding, and as you can see from the photo below, much more water is not needed now...<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S7Zlu7hTl_I/AAAAAAAAAlw/EbRr0SS-S5I/s1600/tulvat_nousee.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="300" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S7Zlu7hTl_I/AAAAAAAAAlw/EbRr0SS-S5I/s400/tulvat_nousee.jpg" width="400" /></a></div>
<div style='clear: both;'></div>

        <div class="date-outer">
      
<h2 class='date-header'><span>Monday, March 8, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='8167948976146404642'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://highlatitudebirdwatcher.blogspot.com/2010/03/in-search-of-woodpeckers-part-4-4.html'>In search of the woodpeckers, part 4 / 4</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
<a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S5VnBcUY0OI/AAAAAAAAAlQ/UsJVuUrtibk/s1600-h/ktikka.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="400" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S5VnBcUY0OI/AAAAAAAAAlQ/UsJVuUrtibk/s400/ktikka.jpg" width="300" /></a>Last Sunday was the last day of my winter vacation. Yet another perfect day for outdoor activities, like birding with snow shoes. Weather was excellent like it had been already for several days. This time my older son Petro joined me and we headed to <a href="http://kansalaisen.karttapaikka.fi/linkki?scale=16000&amp;text=Rauttunniemi+nature+reserve&amp;srs=EPSG%3A3067&amp;y=6788938&amp;mode=rasta&amp;x=337319&amp;lang=en">Rauttunniemi nature reserve</a> and it's surroundings.<br />
<br />
<a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S5VnLQFNWTI/AAAAAAAAAlY/-BOXdat14Aw/s1600-h/homppaup.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="256" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S5VnLQFNWTI/AAAAAAAAAlY/-BOXdat14Aw/s320/homppaup.jpg" width="320" /></a>The first bird worth to mention was the Northern Goshawk <i>(Accipiter gentilis)</i>, which flew over the fields in Vedentaka village while we were still driving to Rauttunniemi. In and near the nature reserve we saw and heard quite a lot of birds, like 5 Long-tailed Tits <i>(Aegithalos caudatus)</i> - a new species for this year, about 10 European Siskins <i>(Carduelis spinus)</i>, 1 Black Woodpecker <i>(Dryocopus martius)</i>, at least 2 Great Spotted Woodpeckers <i>(Dendrocopos major)</i>, two unidentified medium size woodpeckers i.e. either Great Spotted Woodpeckers or White-backed Woodpeckers and much more (tits, greenfiches, crows etc.) White-backed Woodpecker is very rare and endangered species here, but still possible to meet, so you have to always consider that when you are trying identify woodpeckers here.<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
This area is one of my favorite areas around here and I will come birding here later in the spring again.<br />
<br />
<br />
In photos: Great Spotted Woodpecker <i>(Dendrocopos major)</i> above and Willow Tit<i> (Poecile montanus) </i>below.<br />
<span class="std nobr"></span>

<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='5573889128890034739'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://highlatitudebirdwatcher.blogspot.com/2010/03/worlds-rarest-birds-through-lens.html'>The World's rarest birds through the lens</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S5T73c8oY-I/AAAAAAAAAlI/SbVwr_9HTM8/s1600-h/bljuttu.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="141" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S5T73c8oY-I/AAAAAAAAAlI/SbVwr_9HTM8/s400/bljuttu.jpg" width="400" /></a></div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
A new international photo competition covering the world's 623 most threatened birds has just been launched. This is a follow-up to the photo competitions that led to the production of the highly acclaimed Rare Birds Yearbooks 2008 and 2009.<br />
<br />
The photos submitted for the new competition will feature in a landmark publication &#8211; The World's Rarest Birds &#8211; which will be produced by the not-for-profit publisher WILDGuides next year. The proceeds will be donated to BirdLife International's Preventing Extinctions Programme, as was the case with the Rare Birds Yearbooks. <br />
<br />
The World's Rarest Birds will be a lavishly illustrated hardback book, covering the 362 species categorised as Endangered and 65 that are Data Deficient, as well as the 192 Critically Endangered species and the four species that are Extinct in the Wild and only now exist in captivity. It will be a comprehensive directory of the world's most threatened bird species and include specially written feature articles on the key bird conservation issues in each of the world&#8217;s regions.<br />
<br />
Read the whole article from Birdlife International's site: <a href="http://www.birdlife.org/news/news/2010/03/worlds_rarest.html">The World's rarest birds through the lens</a>
<div style='clear: both;'></div>

        <div class="date-outer">
      
<h2 class='date-header'><span>Saturday, March 6, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='1021352140835856412'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://highlatitudebirdwatcher.blogspot.com/2010/03/in-search-of-woodpeckers-parts-2-3.html'>In search of the woodpeckers, parts 2 & 3</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S5KoFTtc_GI/AAAAAAAAAkw/d-sZHtsXuqg/s1600-h/sinitintti.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="320" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S5KoFTtc_GI/AAAAAAAAAkw/d-sZHtsXuqg/s320/sinitintti.jpg" width="276" /></a></div>During last days the weather has been simpy excellent; -5&#176; C, calm and almost no clouds in the sky. Wonderful winter weather full of promises of forthcoming spring. All the birds are singing, sunshine feels warm in your face. Spring equinox is getting closer every day and the days are getting longer and lighter.<br />
<br />
Two days ago we saw another Black Woodpecker <i>(Dryocopus martius)</i>- it flew over the road when we drove to Tampere. This was so far the third Black Woodpecker for me this year. Yesterday I was birding with my older son Petro. We went to <a href="http://kansalaisen.karttapaikka.fi/linkki?scale=8000&amp;text=Jutikkalanharju&amp;srs=EPSG%3A3067&amp;y=6783504&amp;mode=rasta&amp;x=340901&amp;lang=en">Jutikkalanharju</a> (ridge of Jutikkala), a very beautiful ridge in the southern part of Valkeakoski. I was expecting several woodpeckers from there but all we found was a female Great Spotted Woodpecker<i> (Dendrocopos major)</i>. No Grey-headed, Lesser Spotted nor Black Woodpecker, all species that breed in this area. Still we enjoyed the beautiful nature and all the other birds we saw there, like Blue Tits <i>(Cyanistes caeruleus)</i>, Willow Tits <i>(Poecile montanus)</i> and Coal Tits<i> (Periparus ater)</i> who visited in the feeding place of local birder Jussi Kallio. Also couple of Common Ravens <i>(Corvus corax)</i> were passing by.<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S5KoWsGchGI/AAAAAAAAAk4/B2cMaO83-n4/s1600-h/pontikka.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S5KoWsGchGI/AAAAAAAAAk4/B2cMaO83-n4/s320/pontikka.jpg" /></a></div>Today I was birding with my younger son Roni. Our target was <a href="http://kansalaisen.karttapaikka.fi/linkki?scale=8000&amp;text=Heikkil%C3%A4nmets%C3%A4&amp;srs=EPSG%3A3067&amp;y=6793720&amp;mode=rasta&amp;x=340493&amp;lang=en">HeikkilÃ¤nmetsÃ¤ nature reserve</a>, nice old forest close to downtown Valkeakoski. Like yesterday and few days ago, the snowshoes were mandatory again - thick layer of snow covers everything. This third trip convinced me that the Great Spotted Woodpeckers are scarce at the moment - and the reason for that is the fact that there is no cones in spruces. When there is lot's of cones, you can easily find 5-10 or even more Great Spotted Woodpeckers from this forest. Today there was none. But still we were lucky - we found Eurasian Three-toed Woodpecker <i>(Picoides tridactylus)</i>! Very nice, as it's a very secretive species, silent and feeding often high in the spruces. In addition to Three-toed Woodpecker, there was also 5 Eurasian Jays <i>(Garrrulus glandarius)</i>, 2 Crested Tits <i>(Lophophanes cristatus)</i>, 2 Coal Tits <i>(Periparus ater)</i>,  6 Eurasian Bullfinches <i>(Pyrrhula pyrrhula)</i>, Eurasian Treecreeper <i>(Certhia familiaris)</i>, 2 Eurasian Siskins <i>(Carduelis spinus)</i> and several more common birds.<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S5KogzEAY9I/AAAAAAAAAlA/UVnJ9mf9EbY/s1600-h/ttintti.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S5KogzEAY9I/AAAAAAAAAlA/UVnJ9mf9EbY/s320/ttintti.jpg" /></a></div>Tomorrow is the last day of my winter vacation, time for the part 4 of this search which will also be the last part - at least for now. If the nights turn warm, I think I will concentrate on listening the owls...
<div style='clear: both;'></div>
</div>

        <div class="date-outer">
      
<h2 class='date-header'><span>Wednesday, March 3, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='3576329752529737945'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://highlatitudebirdwatcher.blogspot.com/2010/03/in-search-of-woodpeckers-part-1.html'>In search of the woodpeckers, part 1</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"></div><a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S42Op42zshI/AAAAAAAAAkI/1PsYv49JdVs/s1600-h/tulkku.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="272" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S42Op42zshI/AAAAAAAAAkI/1PsYv49JdVs/s640/tulkku.jpg" width="640" /></a><a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S42OtsUxKcI/AAAAAAAAAkQ/0K5Yhyiip7w/s1600/kuva.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S42OtsUxKcI/AAAAAAAAAkQ/0K5Yhyiip7w/s320/kuva.jpg" /></a>As it's winter holiday now, and most of the migrating birds are still somewhere far south, it's time concentrate on local birds that stay here year round. Like woodpeckers. I will try to find during this holiday week all woodpecker species that breed in this region&nbsp; - tough quest, but I will try still.<br />
<br />
But... when you jump out of the car and step out of the road, you will suddenly realise that it's not so easy. At the moment we have about 60 cm of snow and in some place where wind has blown there is much more, even 1,5 meters. So snowshoes or skis are mandatory equipment. Personally I prefer snowshoes because they fit well in my car and they are easy to use in forests, where long skis instead are very difficult.<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S42L0aGRIyI/AAAAAAAAAkA/DAx_tXxCrY4/s1600-h/wphole.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="320" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S42L0aGRIyI/AAAAAAAAAkA/DAx_tXxCrY4/s320/wphole.jpg" width="240" /></a>So today I went to old forest of Mettivuori with my younger son Roni. As you can see from the photo on the left, there certainly are woodpeckers, at least during summer...<br />
<br />
But today we did not hear any knocking of woodpeckers, just a distant call of Black Woodpecker <i>(Dryocopus martius)</i>.A flock of Eurasian Bullfinches <i>(Pyrrhula pyrrhula)</i> were feeding in treetops and some Coal Tits<i> (Periparus ater)</i> and Willow Tits <i>(Poecile montanus)</i> were passing by. One of the male Bullfinches started singing, clear sign of the forthcoming spring.<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S42VALFqlsI/AAAAAAAAAko/8Cp-gseEomE/s1600-h/pkpontto.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S42VALFqlsI/AAAAAAAAAko/8Cp-gseEomE/s320/pkpontto.jpg" /></a>From Mettivuori forest we moved to downtown Valkeakoski, to see if there is any waterfowl in the dowstream of the power plant. There was saw a pair of Common Goldeneyes <i>(Bucephala clangula)</i> and also a pair of Mallards <i>(Anas platyrhychos)</i>. Nice flock of 90 European Greenfiches <i>(Carduelis chloris</i>) were resting in nearby park.<br />
<br />
<br />
Nothing special today, but a nice trip still. On the left is one more photo, an example of the nest box for Eurasian Treecreeper <i>(Certhia familiaris)</i>. As you can see, the entrance to box is in the side - not in the front like in ordinary boxes.
<div style='clear: both;'></div>
</div>

        <div class="date-outer">
      
<h2 class='date-header'><span>Monday, March 1, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='8975804817560123080'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://highlatitudebirdwatcher.blogspot.com/2010/03/garden-warblers-with-mysterious-songs.html'>Garden warblers with mysterious songs  - a call for informations and recordings</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
I'll share this call for informations and recordings:<br />
<br />
<i>Very rarely garden warblers </i><i>(Sylvia borin) sing so atypical songs that you need to see the bird in order to find out, which species you have heard. For years we have tried to collect informations and recordings of such mysteriously singing garden warblers. By now we have got recordings of 31 birds: 16 from Germany, 2 from Holland, 1 from Switzerland, 6 from Denmark, 5 from Sweden, and 3 from Finland. Examples of the songs of the 31 birds can be heard at the site: <a href="http://www.ginster-verlag.de/Raetselsaenger.html">http://www.ginster-verlag.de/Raetselsaenger.html<br />
</a></i><br />
<i>Ten of the 31 birds (category b on the above mentioned site) sang the same type of song, which consisted of very short elements with a rate of 10-15 per second. The overall pattern of each song was a wawing up and down in frequency - a character also found in normal garden warbler songs. Some individuals were recorded in more years. <br />
</i><br />
<i>The mysterious garden warblers have been found in a rather narrow belt, reaching from Finland to southern Germany and Switzerland. Why do some garden warblers sing such peculiar songs - and never normal garden warbler song? Three hypotheses have been forwarded. <br />
<br />
</i><br />
<i>One suggests that the birds have been hatched late in the season, when adult garden warblers have stopped singing. If they have to learn their songs from adults in the first calendar year, they are then forced to develop their singing based on only an &#8222;innate" template. According to this hypothesis, the birds sing a crude model of garden warbler song. This might apply to the 10 birds in group b on the above mentioned site. The remaining birds sing a bit more elaborate songs, which may be because they have had some experience with garden warbler singing. If the hypothesis is true, we might expect the mysterious garden warblers to be found especially in colder climates with shorter breeding seasons. However, one problem with the hypothesis is that we do not know when garden warblers acquire their song models.&nbsp; <br />
<br />
Another hypothesis is based on the observation that one of the individuals did not respond to play back of garden warbler song. The observers concluded that the bird was deaf. The reason for the abnormal songs should thus be, that the birds cannot hear and acquire the necessary song models. However, a number of studies of singing in birds, which have been experimentally deafened at an early age, show, that the birds develop very diffuse song elements (probably due to the lack of auditory feedback). The abnormal garden warblers sing well-defined song elements. That elements appear fuzzy on some recordings is mostly due to the recording quality and/or the acoustic conditions during the recording.&nbsp;&nbsp;</i><br />
<br />
<i>The third hypothesis relate to the geographical distribution of the recorded birds, which as mentioned are found in a rather narrow zone from Finland to Central Europe. It is suggested that two genetically different populations meet in this zone, and that the mysterious singers are &#8222;hybrids" between parents of each genotype. Why these &#8222;hybrids" should develop abnormal songs is unknown, but one suggestion is that they are deaf. However, at present no data are available on the suggested divide between genetically different garden warblers in the zone with abnormally singing garden warblers. <br />
</i><br />
<i>The conclusion must be, that we simply do not know the cause of the abnormal songs. <br />
<br />
Therefore we ask for more observations and recordings.<br />
<br />
1) Have you heard birds with this type of song? Please let us know, and if possible, send us available informations and recordings.<br />
<br />
2) If you happen to meet birds with this type of song in the future, we should like to know all about when and where. Preferably, we should also ask you to record to singing of the bird(s) with whatever available equipment. The most import thing is to get rather close to the bird, so that echoes from the surroundings are reduced.<br />
<br />
3) Is the bird deaf? You may help to solve this question by playing garden warbler alarm (mobbing) calls to the bird. Does it react to the calls? If you need a recording of garden warbler alarm calls, please, contact one of us.<br />
<br />
4) Does the bird attract a mate and produce offspring? All observations on the behaviour of the bird in relation to conspecifics is interesting.<br />
<br />
5) If possible, it would be optimal to ring the bird, so that it could be recognised, if it returns in future years.<br />
<br />
Contact one of us, if you need any information on the project, and if you can help us with informations and recordings.<br />
<br />
Thank you.<br />
<br />
Goetz Rheinwald &amp; Poul Hansen<br />
<br />
Email: goetz.rheinwald @&nbsp; t-online.de<br />
<br />
Email: poulh @&nbsp; nathist.dk</i>
<div style='clear: both;'></div>
</div>

<h2 class='date-header'><span>Sunday, February 28, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='1445465777128114056'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://highlatitudebirdwatcher.blogspot.com/2010/02/red-throated-loon-dipper-and-more.html'>Red-throated Loon, Dipper and more</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
<a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S4qYdPPQZ8I/AAAAAAAAAjw/4ASavtX9TBk/s1600-h/dipper.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="262" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S4qYdPPQZ8I/AAAAAAAAAjw/4ASavtX9TBk/s400/dipper.jpg" width="400" /></a>Winter vacation started, finally some time for birding too. Well, the trip on Saturday the 27th was combined birding and shopping trip with my sons, so we concentrated on easy locations. At first we tried to find Black Grouses <i>(Tetrao tetrix)</i>, but today they were somewhere deeper in the forests, so we let them stay there and drove to Herralankoski rapids in LempÃ¤Ã¤lÃ¤. Good choice, there were 5 Goosanders <i>(Mergus merganser)</i> and 1 Eurasian Dipper <i>(Cinclus cinclus)</i>. Latter was a new one in my year list 2010.<br />
<br />
From LempÃ¤Ã¤lÃ¤ we moved to Tampere. After delicous KÃ¤Ã¤g (aka Kag'h) lunch in best Chinese Grill of Tampere, we drove to EtelÃ¤puisto park, to see the waterfowl of Viinikanlahti bay. It's the best open water area in Pirkanmaa county during winters. Full of Mallards <i>(Anas platyrhynchos)</i> - hundreds of them, 9 Goosanders <i>(Mergus merganser)</i>, Smew<i> (Mergus albellus)</i> and one more for my year list: Red-throated Loon <i>(Gavia stellata) </i>- photo below. Very nice! And just a right time to switch to shopping - phone rang and my wife asked us to pick her up. More birding trips next week, if the weather allows, now there's raining water, sleet and snow...<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S4qdgE1KGsI/AAAAAAAAAj4/_29AL2zaXXI/s1600-h/loon.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="257" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S4qdgE1KGsI/AAAAAAAAAj4/_29AL2zaXXI/s400/loon.jpg" width="400" /></a></div>

<h2 class='date-header'><span>Monday, February 15, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='4818536294321478629'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://highlatitudebirdwatcher.blogspot.com/2010/02/feels-like-spring.html'>Feels like a spring</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S3keOjEZl0I/AAAAAAAAAjo/ZM2B4_7YfzU/s1600-h/telkka.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; cssfloat: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" ct="true" height="400" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S3keOjEZl0I/AAAAAAAAAjo/ZM2B4_7YfzU/s400/telkka.jpg" width="366" /></a></div>Here in Valkeakoski we have still&nbsp;about 45 cm of snow and cold weather, temperatures vary from -6 to -20 &#176;C. Sounds like a winter, does it? But when you get out, you can see and hear the signs of spring. European Greenfinches <em>(Carduelis chloris)</em>, Great Tits <em>(Parus major)</em> and Blue Tits <em>(Cyanistes cyaneus)</em> are all singing. First Goosanders<em> (Mergus merganser)</em> and female Common Goldeneye <em>(Bucephala clangula)</em> have arrived to open streams of Valkeakoski. Probably these waterfowl do not come from very far, I guess they have been wintering somewhere in Baltic Sea. Anyway, every year these first scouts arrive in February, no matter how mild or cold the winter is. I just wonder when the first Gulls will arrive this year...? Soon, if I'm not totally wrong.
<div style='clear: both;'></div>

<h2 class='date-header'><span>Sunday, January 31, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='2101674508867515974'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://highlatitudebirdwatcher.blogspot.com/2010/01/national-backyard-twitching-weekend.html'>National Backyard Twitching Weekend</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S2WuYi71bYI/AAAAAAAAAiw/ntUtk_CAX1E/s1600-h/roni_kiikaroi.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="300" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S2WuYi71bYI/AAAAAAAAAiw/ntUtk_CAX1E/s400/roni_kiikaroi.jpg" width="400" /></a></div><br />
During this weekend we had "Pihabongaus" event in Finland. Pihabongaus could be translated as Backyard Twitching. The organizer of the event is Birdlife Finland and it has a very powerful patron; Tarja Halonen, the President of Finland.<br />
<br />
The event is open for everyone and all one needs to do is to spend one hour in own backyard, some park or other small area. Pihabongaus is not a competition, so the observed number of birds is not decisive. <span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">Main purpose is to alert the public to observe the winter bird feeders and backyard birds and also the neighbouring nature. </span><span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">At the same time Birdlife Finland gets valuable information on the winter bird life of our country.</span><br />
<br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">During 2009 in this same event total of 15 000 people observed birds in 10 000 backyards. I could bet that this year we get even more participants.&nbsp;</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)"><br />
</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">Personally I participated in Pihabongaus for the 5th time. First in Saturday with Roni and today with Petro. On Saturday we saw 13 species, today 10 and in total 14. Nothing special, just the ordinary suburb winter birds. We could have seen some more also today, but our local newspaper Valkeakosken Sanomat wanted to interview us. The article should be in newspaper tomorrow.</span><br />
<br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">The comple lists from both is below:</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)"> </span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">Saturday 30th of January:</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)"><br />
</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">1. Hooded Crow <i>(Corvus cornix)</i> 8</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">2. European Greenfinch <i>(Carduelis chloris)</i>&nbsp; 45</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">3. Great Tit <i>(Parus major)</i> 35</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">4. European Blue Tit<i> (Cyanistes caeruleus)</i> 50</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">5. Coal Tit <i>(Periparus ater)</i> 1</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">6. Common Magpie <i>(Pica pica) </i>7</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">7. House Sparrow <i>(Passer domesticus) </i>14</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">8. Eurasian Tree Sparrow <i>(Passer montanus)</i> 2</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">9. Eurasian Bullfinch<i> (Pyrrhula pyrrhula)</i> 2</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">10. Great Spotted Woodpecker <i>(Dendrocopos major) </i>1</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">11. Eurasian Siskin<i> (Carduelis spinus)</i> 1</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">12. Common Redpoll <i>(Carduelis flammea)</i> 20 </span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">13. Common Blackbird<i> (Turdus merula) </i>1</span><br />
<br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">Sunday 31st of January:</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)"> </span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">1. Hooded Crow <i>(Corvus cornix)</i> 9</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">2. European Greenfinch <i>(Carduelis chloris)</i>&nbsp; 45</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">3. Great Tit <i>(Parus major)</i> 15</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">4. European Blue Tit<i> (Cyanistes caeruleus)</i> 30<br />
5. Common Magpie <i>(Pica pica) </i>5</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">6. House Sparrow <i>(Passer domesticus) </i>19</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">7. Eurasian Tree Sparrow <i>(Passer montanus)</i> 4</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)">8. Great Spotted Woodpecker <i>(Dendrocopos major) </i>2<br />
9. Common Blackbird<i> (Turdus merula) </i>1<br />
10. Yellowhammer<i> (Emberiza citrinella)</i> 1</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)"><br />
</span><br />
<span onmouseout="_tipoff()" onmouseover="_tipon(this)"><br />

<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='2586370774540375160'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://highlatitudebirdwatcher.blogspot.com/2010/01/better-winter-birding-day.html'>A better winter birding day</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S2Wh4Puv-II/AAAAAAAAAiY/8gILiNVpzSc/s1600-h/uivelo.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" height="260" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S2Wh4Puv-II/AAAAAAAAAiY/8gILiNVpzSc/s400/uivelo.jpg" width="400" /></a></div><br />
On 24th of January I made an urban birding trip with my younger son Roni. Our first target was KoukkujÃ¤rvi dump in Nokia. Disgusting smell, especially in the area of organic waste - but lots of birds. 1500 Western Jackdaws<i> (Corvus monedula)</i>, 50 Common Magpies <i>(Pica pica)</i>, 30 Hooded Crows <i>(Corvus cornix) </i>and 6 Common Ravens <i>(Corvus corax)</i> were the most visible ones. Also lot's of smaller ones - a very nice mixed flock of 120 European Greenfinches <i>(Carduelis chloris)</i>, 20 Common Redpolls <i>(Carduelis flammea)</i>, 1 Arctic Redpoll <i>(Carduelis hornemanni)</i>, 4 Twites<i> (Carduelis flavirostris) </i>and 4 Eurasian Tree Sparrows <i>(Passer montanus).</i> Both Twite and Arctic Redpoll were lifers for Roni, so the boy was very happy! In addition to previous, there was also lonely Fieldfare <i>(Turdus pilaris)</i> and Great Spotted Woodpecker <i>(Dendrocopos major).</i><br />
<br />
From Nokia we drove to Tampere and tried to find White-throated Dipper <i>(Cinclus cinclus)</i> from PÃ¤rrinkoski rapids, but unfortunately the rapids were totally frozen, no open water at all, so no room for Dippers. Shame... Small hightlight was still the fresh footprints of the European Otter <i>(Lutra lutra)</i>, although we did not see the animal.<br />
<br />
<a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S2Wift4Y6WI/AAAAAAAAAig/uJlpJr_tC14/s1600-h/sotka.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S2Wift4Y6WI/AAAAAAAAAig/uJlpJr_tC14/s320/sotka.jpg" /></a>After that we drove to city center of Tampere, where the Tammerkoski flows so fast that there's always some open water. Lot's of Mallards <i>(Anas platyrhynchos)</i> , two Goosanders <i>(Mergus merganser)</i>, 1 female Long-tailed Duck aka Oldsquaw <i>(Clangula hyemalis)</i>, 1 male Tufted Duck <i>(Aythya fuligula)</i> and 1 elegant male Smew<i> (Mergus albellus)</i>.<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S2WioR6nULI/AAAAAAAAAio/PBxYxaju2QY/s1600-h/alli.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S2WioR6nULI/AAAAAAAAAio/PBxYxaju2QY/s320/alli.jpg" /></a></div><br />
<br />
IMHO, Long-tailed Duck is a very dull name for that beautiful arctic duck, I prefer much more the old American name Oldsquaw!<br />
<br />
At this point it was already afternoon, so it was time get back to home. Roni was very happy and so was daddy!
<div style='clear: both;'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-footer'>
<div class='post-footer-line post-footer-line-1'><span class='post-author vcard'>
Posted by
<span class='fn'>Olli</span>
</span>

<h2 class='date-header'><span>Wednesday, January 27, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='3701168835492703162'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://remotebirding.blogspot.com/'>Remote birding - what does it mean?</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
If you read this, you'll probably know what birding means - yes, it's watching the birds i.e birdwatching. But remote birding?<br /><p> Well, in remote birding you don't need binoculars, scope nor camera, but you must have internet connection and TV to watch birds.<br /><br />So, I'm trying to see and identify birds in <b>live TV broadcasts</b> and through <b>live webcams</b>. I will not pay any attention to recordings or movies, all the birds must be out there somewhere right at the same moment when I see them. I'm also trying to capture photos of all the birds, whenever it's possible.<br /><br />At this moment this is just an experiment - I'm just curious on how many bird species I can see and identify via cables!</p>For this experiment I created a new blog: <a href="http://remotebirding.blogspot.com/">Remote birding</a><br /><br />Welcome to remote birding, it's really fun and educational; my identification skills have developed fast again.
<div style='clear: both;'></div>
</div>

<h2 class='date-header'><span>Saturday, January 23, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='9142610418638892653'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://highlatitudebirdwatcher.blogspot.com/2010/01/not-much-birds-around.html'>Not much birds around</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
This winter has been pretty cold compared to last years, almost perfect. Just almost, as I would love to get more snow, now we have just about 30 cm of it. But birds are scarce and it's difficult to find new species to year list. Still I love this winter, this is much better than those wet, dark, rainy and foggy winters, that we have experienced during this millenium.<br />
<br />
But the cold weather itself is not the only reason for the scarcity of birds. Low vole populations have decreased the number of owls and many hawks, as well as the number of Great Gray Shrikes <i>(Lanius excubitor)</i><i></i>. Drainage of water in lakes is minimal this winter and most of the open water locations are now covered with ice, so waterfowl are scarce too. Rowan berries are almost finished so most of the Bohemian Waxwings <i>(Bombycilla garrulus)</i> and Fieldfares <i>(Turdus pilaris)</i> are disappearing to south. And in addition to above, during last summer, due to cold and wet weather, many birds failed in their nesting, so the numbers of many tits and other small birds are also low. In fact so low, that I have not seen any Coal, Crested nor Willow Tits in our backyard this winter - this is the first winter ever that this happens!<br />
<br />
Well, for tomorrow I'm planning a birding trip to Tampere and Nokia, there should be some nice species that I have not seen for a while. Let's see if I'm lucky or not?<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S1sO2imvV2I/AAAAAAAAAcs/SjURCnmswG8/s1600-h/puukiipija.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S1sO2imvV2I/AAAAAAAAAcs/SjURCnmswG8/s320/puukiipija.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<i>Eurasian Treecreeper (Certhia familiaris) feeding in our backyard</i>

<h2 class='date-header'><span>Monday, January 4, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='3455209873556218681'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://highlatitudebirdwatcher.blogspot.com/2010/01/winter-bird-rally-2010.html'>Winter Bird Rally 2010</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S0HBtrJ21GI/AAAAAAAAAbU/xmsz_7L7Xpc/s1600-h/rallipojat.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S0HBtrJ21GI/AAAAAAAAAbU/xmsz_7L7Xpc/s400/rallipojat.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
Winter Bird Rally 2010 of Valkeakosken Lintuharrastajat was held this year on the 2nd of January in rather arctic weather. The morning temperature was -20&#176; C and it rose only to -18&#176; C during the day. No signs of global warming, which was in fact very refreshing after several warm winters. Basically birding in arctic weather is just the matter of proper clothing, but naturally there is some challenges, like the fact that your binocular lenses get easily covered by ice when they first get moist in warm car.<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
Our rally team "Suur-Akaan Nuijamiehet" was the same that we have had for many years; <a href="http://www.facebook.com/#/profile.php?id=100000137202070">Ville TyÃ¶ppÃ¶nen</a>, Heikki-Pekka Innala, Jonne MÃ¤kelÃ¤ and <a href="http://www.facebook.com/#/olli.haukkovaara">undersigned</a>.<br />
<br />
The rally time was 6:00 - 16:00, basically 10 hours. But this year we started only at 8:30, as we knew that it was totally hopeless to search for owls, due to fact that the vole population cycle is right now in low phase. The sun rose at 9:38 and set down at 15:18, so the effective birding time was about 7 hours and 15 minutes.<br />
<br />
At first we headed to couple of feeding places in Valkeakoski suburbs. The first species, Common Redpoll <i>(Carduelis flammea) </i>was observed at 8:44. During next 16 minutes we observed 10 species but after that the rate slowed down.<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S0HB20QstaI/AAAAAAAAAbc/LKPcrRBaRIo/s1600-h/peltopyyt.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S0HB20QstaI/AAAAAAAAAbc/LKPcrRBaRIo/s400/peltopyyt.jpg" /></a><br />
</div>During the day we drove around the southern parts of Pirkanmaa county, trying to find especially grouses and woodpeckers. We were lucky with grouses; we found the Black Grouse <i>(Tetrao tetrix</i>), Grey Partridge <i>(Perdix perdix)</i> and Common Pheasant <i>(Phasianus colchicus)</i>. But the woodpeckers were not favourable for us; just the Great Spotted Woodpecker <i>(Dendrocopos major) </i>and Lesser Spotted Woodpecker <i>(Denrocopos minor)</i>. The latter was in fact a highlight for us and turned out to be an ace when we compared our results with other teams. Grey Partridge was also very good and in fact this was the first time ever that this species has been observed in Winter Bird Rally of Valkeakosken Lintuharrastajat!<br />
<br />
Waterfowl were scarce. Drainage of water in lakes has been minimal this winter and most of the open water locations were now covered with ice. Many waterfowl and 1 Grey Heron <i>(Ardea cinerea)</i> are concentrated in the condensation water pool of United Paper Mills in Valkeakoski, but unfortunately that area is nowadays out of limits, so all rally teams missed couple of species this year. Still we managed to find an adult Whooper Swan <i>(Cygnus cygnus)</i>, male Goosander <i>(Mergus merganser)</i> and Great Crested Grebe <i>(Podiceps cristatus)</i> from the few open water rapids and straits of LempÃ¤Ã¤lÃ¤.<br />
<br />
The obligatory drawback of this rally for us was the Merlin <i>(Falco columbarius)</i>. I found a fast flying bird over the forest in Ritvala; Heikki-Pekka managed to see it, but Jonne and Ville missed it. So, as the rules say that over 50% of the team members must see and identify the bird, the Merlin was not acceptable... <br />
<br />
Like always in these rallies, the first morning hours are the best. During afternoon we got only couple of new species and at 15:47, we ticked the last species, the Eurasian Treecreeper <i>(Certhia familiaris)</i> from my own backyard.<br />
<br />
<a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S0HCktKZadI/AAAAAAAAAbk/H2nNeD6cWaU/s1600-h/olli_ralli.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S0HCktKZadI/AAAAAAAAAbk/H2nNeD6cWaU/s400/olli_ralli.jpg" /></a>After hard but fun day, our result was 34 species. The winner team got 35, so we were left to silver - exactly like last year. Total number of all species observed by all teams was 42, which is 14 species less than last year. Five species were aces, i.e. seen only by one team: Eurasian Siskin <i>(Carduelis spinus)</i>, Lesser Spotted Woodpecker <i>(Denrocopos minor)</i>, White-backed Woodpecker <i>(Dendrocopos leucotos)</i>, Grey Partridge <i>(Perdix perdix)</i> and Great Grey Shrike <i>(Lanius excubitor)</i>. Most teams drove 100-160 kilometers, our team together with winners had the longest drives; 203 kilometers in both team.<br />
<br />
Next year again - and then we will take the gold position back to us!<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
And below is the complete list of the species we saw, including the Merlin:<br />
<br />
Great Crested Grebe <i>(Podiceps cristatus)</i> <br />
Whooper Swan <i>(Cygnus cygnus)</i><br />
Goosander <i>(Mergus merganser)</i><br />
Mallard <i>(Anas platyrhynchos)</i><br />
Merlin<i> (Falco columbarius)</i><br />
Eurasian Sparrowhawk <i>(Accipiter nisus)</i><br />
Black Grouse <i>(Tetrao tetrix)</i><br />
Common Pheasant <i>(Phasianus colchicus)</i><br />
Grey Partridge <i>(Perdix perdix)</i><br />
Great Spotted Woodpecker <i>(Dendrocopos major)</i><br />
Lesser Spotted Woodpecker <i>(Dendrocopos minor)</i><br />
Feral Pigeon <i>(Columba livia)</i><br />
Eurasian Collared Dove <i>(Streptopelia decaocto)</i><br />
Great Tit <i>(Parus major)</i><br />
European Blue Tit <i>(Cyanistes caeruleus)</i><br />
Crested Tit <i>(Lophophanes cristatus)</i><br />
Coal Tit<i> (Periparus ater)</i><br />
Willow Tit<i> (Poecile montanus)</i><br />
Goldcrest <i>(Regulus regulus)</i><br />
Bohemian Waxwing <i>(Bombycilla garrulus)</i><br />
Common Blackbird <i>(Turdus merula)</i><br />
Fieldfare<i> (Turdus pilaris)&nbsp;</i><br />
Common Raven <i>(Corvus corax) </i><br />
Hooded Crow <i>(Corvus corone cornix)</i><br />
Western Jackdaw <i>(Corvus monedula)</i> <br />
Common Magpie <i>(Pica pica)</i><br />
Eurasian Jay <i>(Garrulus glandarius)</i><br />
Eurasian Bullfinch <i>(Pyrrhula pyrrhula)</i><br />
House Sparrow <i>(Passer domesticus) </i><br />
Eurasian Tree Sparrow <i>(Passer montanus) </i><br />
European Greenfinch <i>(Carduelis chloris)</i><br />
Common Redpoll <i>(Carduelis flammea)</i><br />
Yellowhammer <i>(Emberiza citrinella)</i>

<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='6049140924261785186'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://highlatitudebirdwatcher.blogspot.com/2010/01/birding-in-seattle-and-snoqualmie.html'>Birding in Seattle and Snoqualmie National Forest, Washington, USA, 3-4/11/2009</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S0G34N5zRNI/AAAAAAAAAbE/Lp32FKrD8k4/s1600-h/vuori640.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S0G34N5zRNI/AAAAAAAAAbE/Lp32FKrD8k4/s640/vuori640.jpg" /></a><br />
</div>Abstract:<br />
<br />
<span style="font-size: x-small;">My trip to Seattle and Snoqualmie National Forest was not a pure birding trip, but a combined vacation / geocaching / birding trip, as I primarily travelled to Seattle to meet my old friends. Still I was able to get some new lifers and enjoy the nature of Cascades. I was lucky with the weather, perfect sunshine and almost no wind at all. If you ever visit this area, please note that the weather is often very wet there, so waterproof clothing is really necessary. It&#8217;s also good to know that many National Forests in Oregon and Washington require day use fees. Please see the links in bottom of this article for more information.</span><br />
<br />
Day 1. 3/11/2009<br />
<br />
I arrived to USA from Canada by Amtrak Coach. I was hoping to see some owl during evening travel to Seattle, but no luck this time. In Seattle I met my friends Bryan and Heidi Roth, had a nice dinner with them. No bird observations yet, only one mammal, a Raccoon <i>(Procyon lotor)</i> at Capitol Hill.<br />
<br />
Day 2. 4/11/2009<br />
<br />
I woke up at 6:30 a.m. at <a href="http://www.bbcapitolhill.com/">Bed &amp; Breakfast Capitol Hill</a>, very nice place, I can really recommend it! After breakfast I walked 4,8 kilometres from Capitol Hill to Fremont. Nice walk, but no lifers; Pacific Wren <i>(Troglodytes pacificus)</i>, Feral Pigeons <i>(Columba livia)</i>, Common Starlings <i>(Sturnus vulgaris)</i>, Chickadees, some large gulls, probably Glaucous-winged or California Gulls, singing Spotted Towhee <i>(Pipilo maculatus)</i>, some American Crows <i>(Corvus brachyrhynchos)</i> and some thrush, probably American Robin <i>(Turdus migratorius)</i>. I tried to find the Belted Kingfisher <i>(Ceryle alcyon)</i> from marina of the Lake Union, but had do luck with it here either (tried to find it from Point Roberts earlier).<br />
<br />
Later in the morning I was on the highway 90 with <a href="http://www.facebook.com/#/jeremyirish">Jeremy Irish</a> and Kelly Bente, heading to Snoqualmie National Forest. Final location was close to Annette Lake Trail where our target, an extraordinary geocache was. Well, that&#8217;s not in the scope of this blog, so I will not write about it more here.<br />
<br />
Annette Lake Trail is a serpentine path in the slopes of unnamed mountain between Humpback Mountain and Mt Catherine. Like in all northern hemisphere forests in this time of year, these forests were also quite silent. But not totally, couple of Golden-crowned Kinglet <i>(Regulus satrapa)</i> flocks were feeding in tree tops, also Black-capped Chickadees <i>(Poecile atricapillus)</i> and Chestnut-backed Chickadees (Poecile rufescens) were here and there.<br />
<br />
American Dipper <i>(Cinclus mexicanus)</i> was on my wishlist - I tried to spot it from the river that was running down from the mountain. The river looked perfect, but no dipper anywhere, bummer...!<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S0G4A02tMYI/AAAAAAAAAbM/DVzoP0hMryM/s1600-h/joki.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/S0G4A02tMYI/AAAAAAAAAbM/DVzoP0hMryM/s400/joki.jpg" /></a><br />
</div>I was also hoping for some Grouse, but no, no luck with them either. I was also looking for Pileated Woodpecker <i>(Dryocopus pileatus)</i>. There were fresh marks of its existence; lots of big holes in dead trees, exactly similar tracks that our Black Woodpecker <i>(Dryocopus martius)</i> makes. But the king of North American woodpeckers was hiding somewhere else this time. Shame&#8230;<br />
<br />
Ten minutes later I heard the sounds of Crossbills. But... I was expecting Red Crossbills <i>(Loxia curvirostra)</i>, but these sounds did definitely not belong to them. White-winged Crossbills <i>(Loxia leucoptera)</i>! I found the birds, 3 of them, from treetops. Nice record! I&#8217;m familiar with this species from Finland, but even there where I live, it&#8217;s very irregular and I had not seen the species for years. When I look at the maps at Sibley, this was probably a good observation also in the State of Washington.<br />
<br />
After few miles we were in the location of the geocache that we were looking for. While investigating its content, I heard an interesting sound close to us. A woodpecker, no doubt about it, but which one? This time it was Downy Woodpecker <i>(Picoides pubescens)</i>, the smallest of North American woodpeckers. Nice bird indeed and a lifer to me. But suddenly the sound changed, woodpecker went quiet but some other bird was calling. I could not see it well; just some medium sized bird flew briefly between spruces. It was a Jay&#8230; and I had and idea which Jay it could be. Later I found exactly the same sound from <a href="http://www.xeno-canto.org/species.php?query=perisoreus+canadensis">Xeno-canto America</a>, it was Gray Jay <i>(Perisoreus canadensis)</i>. Lifer again, but what a shame that I did not see it better, as I really like these Jays! Close cousin of Gray Jay, the Siberian Jay <i>(Perisoreus infaustus)</i>, is one of my favourite birds in Finland.<br />
<br />
While trekking back to the car, a large group of Chickadees were feeding in nearby spruces. One of the birds looked different; it was Mountain Chickadee <i>(Poecile gambeli)</i>! There I heard another familiar sound &#8211; a Treecreeper, but in this case it was the American cousin of our Treecreeper, the Brown Creeper (<i>Certhia americana)</i>. I also saw the bird briefly but well enough. Two more lifers!<br />
<br />
When we drove back to Seattle, I got the last lifer; Stellers Jay <i>(Cyanocitta stelleri)</i>. It landed to a tree in my eye-level right next to highway. What a beautiful bird it was!<br />
<br />
Closer to Seattle I saw still one Red-tailed Hawk <i>(Buteo jamaicensis)</i>. And in the evening, after sunset when I had dinner with Jeremy &amp; Samsy Irish at <a href="http://www.rays.com/">Ray&#8217;s Boathouse</a>, Great Blue Heron <i>(Ardea herodias)</i> was resting right next to restaurant windows, in top of pole. Nice end for the day.<br />
<br />
Early next morning I started my flight back to Finland&#8230; I really miss Seattle and the mountain forests&#8230;<br />
<br />
Must get back there some day, with my whole family. <br />
<b><br />
<br />
More information:</b><br />
<br />
Annette Lake Trail: <a href="http://www.wta.org/go-hiking/hikes/annette-lake">http://www.wta.org/go-hiking/hikes/annette-lake</a><br />
<br />
Snoqualmie National Forest: <a href="http://www.fs.fed.us/r6/mbs/">http://www.fs.fed.us/r6/mbs/</a><br />
<br />
Seattle Audubon Society: <a href="http://www.seattleaudubon.org/sas/">http://www.seattleaudubon.org/sas/</a> <br />
<br />
Checklist of Washington State birds: <a href="http://www.wos.org/WAList01.htm">http://www.wos.org/WAList01.htm</a>
