        <div class="date-outer">
      
<h2 class='date-header'><span>Wednesday, November 25, 2009</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='7291069789745873151'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://highlatitudebirdwatcher.blogspot.com/2009/11/birding-in-vancouver-canada-2910.html'>Birding in Vancouver, Canada 29.10 - 3.11.2009</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpSYvKyvvI/AAAAAAAAAZA/R9z3_-7YBMc/s1600/vancouver.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpSYvKyvvI/AAAAAAAAAZA/R9z3_-7YBMc/s640/vancouver.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
Abstract:<br />
<br />
<span style="font-size: x-small;">My trip to Vancouver was not a traditional birding trip, but a business trip, as I flew to Vancouver to participate in a congress. Anyway, I had enough free time to go birding too, so hopefully this trip report gives readers some hints how to find birds in the Vancouver area. At least from the Finnish point of view, Vancouver and its surroundings is a very interesting area, with several species that are hard to see in Europe. A car is almost mandatory and if you don't arrive by your own car, you can hire one from several car rentals either from airport or town centre. Please note also that waterproof boots or shoes would make your birding experience here much more enjoyable &#8211; it rains in this area often and a lot... Also a local guide is worth gold, see birdingpal.org to find one. And what comes to birders in this area &#8211; they are really spoiled in the number of birds and species that can be found in the area; there are about 400 species on the Vancouver checklist, two thirds of the Canada total of 625. For me this visit was perfect, both from professional and birder point of view. Learned new things in congress, got 44 lifers, unforgettable experiences and new friends.</span> <br />
<br />
Day 1. 29/10/2009<br />
<br />
Arrived late in the evening; totally dark outside and heavy rain... so typical weather for British Columbia at this time of year. Just like in Finland. Not a very promising start...<br />
<br />
Day 2. 30/10/2009<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwzHn0LSvZI/AAAAAAAAAa4/ugdepQUnYwo/s1600/09-10-30_Olli+Haukkovaara.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwzHn0LSvZI/AAAAAAAAAa4/ugdepQUnYwo/s640/09-10-30_Olli+Haukkovaara.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: left;"><br />
</div><i><span style="font-size: x-small;">Photo-collage&nbsp;copyright Susan Lindenberge, please click the photo to see the whole collage </span></i><br />
<br />
Woke up at 06:30, when some gull was calling outside my window. Definitely a big gull, sounded like our Herring Gulls, but with a bit different tone. But it was still too dark to identify the bird, even though it was relatively close to my room. After breakfast I went out to see what that gull was. It turned out be an adult Glaucous-Winged Gull <i>(Larus glaucescens)</i>, the most common gull in the area, at least this time of year. Also several Nothwestern Crows <i>(Corvus caurinus)</i> were flying by. First two lifers ticked!<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swo7ZrVvcVI/AAAAAAAAAXA/XT4DZQp0zBI/s1600/Larus_glaucescens.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swo7ZrVvcVI/AAAAAAAAAXA/XT4DZQp0zBI/s320/Larus_glaucescens.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<span style="font-size: x-small;"><br />
</span><br />
<span style="font-size: x-small;"><i>Glaucous-winged Gull (Larus glaucescens) </i></span><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
Susan and Jim Lindenberger, a local birdingpal couple, picked me up from my hotel at sunrise. The weather was moist, slight rain, but fortunately it did not last long and the rest of the day the weather was simply fine.<br />
<br />
At first, we headed to Stanley Park, which is an evergreen oasis of 400 hectares close to the Vancouver downtown core. It can be conveniently accessed by bus, car, or on foot from downtown. It is a birders paradise; over 230 species of birds are readily viewed and heard in its diverse landscape of forests, wetlands and seashore.<br />
<br />
When we arrived on the border of the park a lone goose flew over the car - a Snow Goose <i>(Anser caerulescens)</i>, a lifer and the first Snow Goose of my trip, but definitely not the last... <br />
<br />
At first we headed to Beaver Lake, which is a medium size pond in the middle of coniferous forest. For me the forest itself was amazing with all the majestic cedar, hemlock and fir trees. The first bird sounds I heard there were somehow really familiar to me... only later I realised that they belonged to Golden-crowned Kinglet <i>(Regulus satrapa)</i>, a close relative to our Goldcrest <i>(Regulus regulus)</i>. Also the sounds of Black-capped Chickadees <i>(Poecile atricapilla)</i> and Chestnut-backed Chickadees <i>(Poecile rufescens)</i> were close to European Tits, especially to Willow Tit <i>(Poecile montanus)</i>. <br />
<br />
The walk inside the forest and along the shore of Beaver Lake gave me several lifers. In addition to Golden-crowned Kinglet and Chickadees, new lifers were Spotted Towhees <i>(Pipilo maculatus)</i>, Song Sparrows <i>(Melospiza melodia)</i>, Northern Flicker <i>(Colaptes auratus)</i>, several Pacific Wrens <i>(Troglodytes pacificus)</i> and American Robin <i>(Turdus migratorius)</i>. 8 more lifers! Susan fed the Chickadees and they came to her hand to get sunflower seeds. Also Towhees and Sparrows were very tame and curious. Other birds we saw there were 3 Common Ravens <i>(Corcus corax)</i>, Great Blue Heron <i>(Ardea herodias), </i>lots of Nothwestern Crows and Mallards <i>(Anas platyrhynchos)</i> and a pair of Wood Ducks<i> (Aix sponsa)</i>.<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swo7lqJqEtI/AAAAAAAAAXI/5FBXMBKNynM/s1600/Parus+rufescens.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swo7lqJqEtI/AAAAAAAAAXI/5FBXMBKNynM/s320/Parus+rufescens.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<span style="font-size: x-small;"><i>Chestnut-backed Chickadee </i></span><i><span style="font-size: x-small;">(Poecile rufescens) on Susan's hand</span><br />
</i><br />
<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swo7y0HlMhI/AAAAAAAAAXQ/5cWOYDKYO6A/s1600/Pipilo+maculatus.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swo7y0HlMhI/AAAAAAAAAXQ/5cWOYDKYO6A/s320/Pipilo+maculatus.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<span style="font-size: x-small;"><i>Spotted Towhee </i><i>(Pipilo maculatus)</i></span><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
Next stop was Lost Lagoon. This pond was different, less grass and more urban. Walk around the pond was successful; 7 new lifers and a closer look at many previously seen birds. Double-crested Cormorants <i>(Phalacrocorax auritus)</i>, Buffleheads <i>(Bucephala albeola)</i>, American Coots <i>(Fulica americana)</i>, American Wigeons <i>(Anas americana)</i>, Canada Geese <i>(Branta canadensis)</i> but some subspecies (Aleutian or Richadson&#8217;s) that is a bit smaller than the big one (Dusky) we have in Finland, 3 Canvasbacks <i>(Aythya valisineria)</i>,&nbsp; Mallards, Glaucous-winged Gulls again, California Gulls <i>(Larus californicus)</i>, some hybrid Gulls (probably <i>californicus x glaucescens</i> or <i>occidentalis x glaucescens</i>), Song Sparrows again, Fox Sparrow <i>(Passerella iliaca)</i>, Dark Eyed Junco <i>(Junco hyemalis)</i>, close look at both Ruby-crowned Kinglet <i>(Regulus calendula)</i> and Golden Crowned Kinglet, 1 Red-winged Blackbird <i>(Agelaius phoeniceus)</i>, 1 Varied Thrush <i>(Ixoreus naevius)</i> and Great Blue Heron. Very nice walk!<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swoz3qrAU5I/AAAAAAAAAWo/4yhWQ1yLqsM/s1600/hybrid_gull.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swoz3qrAU5I/AAAAAAAAAWo/4yhWQ1yLqsM/s320/hybrid_gull.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<span style="font-size: x-small;"><i>Left: Glaucous-winged Gull (Larus glaucescens)<br />
Right: some hybrid gull (Larus glaucescens x occidentalis ???)</i></span><br />
<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><br />
</div><div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swo8BQFVJJI/AAAAAAAAAXY/mdHn5nJjL40/s1600/Melospiza+melodia.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swo8BQFVJJI/AAAAAAAAAXY/mdHn5nJjL40/s320/Melospiza+melodia.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<i><span style="font-size: x-small;">Song Sparrow (Melospiza melodia)</span> </i><br />
<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><br />
</div><div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swo8N45QZEI/AAAAAAAAAXg/cKbVeUmrRdo/s1600/ardea_heroides_1.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swo8N45QZEI/AAAAAAAAAXg/cKbVeUmrRdo/s320/ardea_heroides_1.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<span style="font-size: x-small;"><i>Great Blue Heron (Ardea heroides) </i></span><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
After this we drove around the whole of Stanley Park along the Park Drive. Next longer stop was at Brockton Point, a cape between Coal Harbour and Burrard Inlet. Susan and Jim had heard that there should be Pelagic Cormorants here, but all we got in the scope were Double-crested Cormorants far away, until finally - just when we were about to leave, we saw a cormorant fishing very close to us. Pelagic Cormorant <i>(Phalacrocorax pelagicus)</i>! Nice! There were also about 15 Ring-billed Gulls <i>(Larus delawarensis)</i> pretty close to us and just when we were finally about to leave, I heard an odd sound from the forest. A loud call, reminding me of something, but could not figure out what it was. Suddenly the bird flew past us, an adult Bald Eagle <i>(Halieaeetus leucocephalus)</i>, so beautiful and magnificent and so close. I just stared at it and forgot to take photos... well, that happens to me too often, I just stop to enjoy birds and forget the photographing. Well, three new lifers from Brockton Point.<br />
<br />
Prospect Point, near to the Lions&nbsp;Gate Bridge,&nbsp;was the next stop. From this point there's a great view over the Burrard Inlet. More cormorants, both Double-crested and Pelagic, gulls and crows, but not much more. Except one more lifer; a small flock of Bushtits <i>(Psaltriparus minimus)</i>. From Prospect Point we drove towards Ferguson Point. Before the destination I spotted something interesting in a road side tree. Jim stopped the car and Susan and I jumped out. Red-Breasted Nuthatch <i>(Sitta canadensis)</i>, cool! Two more lifers this time.<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swo8mc2kOfI/AAAAAAAAAXo/aL2igfDjy5c/s1600/Haematopus+bachmani.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swo8mc2kOfI/AAAAAAAAAXo/aL2igfDjy5c/s320/Haematopus+bachmani.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<span style="font-size: x-small;"><i>American Black Oystercatcher </i><i>(Haematopus bachmani</i></span><br />
<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swo_xkKthmI/AAAAAAAAAYA/k1Bvtp9Zu3o/s1600/ardea_heroides_2.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swo_xkKthmI/AAAAAAAAAYA/k1Bvtp9Zu3o/s320/ardea_heroides_2.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<i><span style="font-size: x-small;">Great Blue Heron (Ardea heroides) at Ferguson Point</span> </i><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
Ferguson Point was a bit similar to Prospect Point, high above sea level. Further in the southeast there was a big flock of Surf Scoters <i>(Melanitta perspicillata)</i>, a species that I had been waiting for years. Cool! Down on the beach I saw a wader; it was the American Black Oystercatcher <i>(Haematopus bachmani)</i>, another lifer! And right above our heads, one more Great Blue Heron was walking on a tree branch. We did not spend much time here but drove closer to the Scoters. Very nice view, as you can see from the photo! In total there were about 530 Surf Scoters, 1 White-winged Scoter <i>(Melanitta deglandi) </i>-a lifer also, 1 Common Loon <i>(Gavia immer)</i> and 4 Barrow&#8217;s Goldeneyes <i>(Bucephala islandica)</i> which was also a lifer to me.<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swo-44_hEyI/AAAAAAAAAX4/Yd2evciiy2w/s1600/melanitta+perspicillata.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swo-44_hEyI/AAAAAAAAAX4/Yd2evciiy2w/s640/melanitta+perspicillata.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><span style="font-size: x-small;"><i>Surf Scoters (Melanitta perspicillata) </i></span><br />
<br />
That's all about Stanley Park, the first &#8220;must see place&#8221; for birders visiting Vancouver. So, what next? Vancouver and its surroundings are located in a huge, I mean really huge delta of the Fraser River. The landscape is naturally very flat and the horizon is dominated by the nearby mountains. Most of the delta is nowadays farmland, lots of fields and pastures. But as you may guess, there must be also wetlands in this area. We drove over the wide fields where Hen Harriers aka Northern Harriers <i>(Circus cyaneus)</i> and Red-tailed Hawks <i>(Buteo jamaicensis)</i> were hunting. Common Starlings <i>(Sturnus vulgaris)</i>, that were introduced to North America late 1800s, were also common. <br />
<br />
So, we drove south via Highway 99 from Vancouver and then turned southwest at Delta towards Ladner. There we turned northwest to Westham Island. The landscape was very similar as the coastal area of Ostrobothnia aka Pohjanmaa in Finland or Maatsalu area in Estonia. Totally flat, lots of fields and pastures, small forests here and there.<br />
<br />
Somewhere in Ladner, I saw a flock of gulls on the roof of a warehouse and asked Jim to stop. Mostly California Gulls, some Glaucous-winged gulls and one very interesting looking 1st year gull. It was clearly larger than California Gulls, about same size as Glaucous-winged gulls. Still it was stockier, and it had a thick-tipped, slightly drooping heavy bill. The bill was black with pale base. Eyes were dark. Head, body and tail were dark brown but breast, throat and head were still somewhat lighter than back. Legs were dark gray with a bit of pinkish overtone. All matched to a new lifer: Western Gull <i>(Larus occidentalis)</i>! Nice! Especially as Glaucous-winged gulls interbreed here extensively with Western, Herring and Glaucous Gulls.<br />
<br />
I mentioned in the start of this article that the lonely Snow Goose that I saw in Stanley Park was the first Snow Goose of my trip, but definitely not the last. When we arrived at Westham Island, I saw something white on the front left. Gulls in a field? No, thousands of Snow Geese. Difficult to count even from the photos but probably some 10 000 - 15 000 and more big flocks arrived all the time from north. And in addition to geese, we noticed a hawk flying around over the fields - Peregrine Falcon <i>(Falco peregrinus)</i>, my favourite bird number one! Cool, I love this place!<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpTJ34slqI/AAAAAAAAAZI/sL7fnEF-Vec/s1600/anser+caerulescens.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpTJ34slqI/AAAAAAAAAZI/sL7fnEF-Vec/s400/anser+caerulescens.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<span style="font-size: x-small;"><i>Small part of the huge Snow Goose (Anser caerulescens) flock </i></span><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
Our target in Westham Island was the Reifel Migratory Bird Sanctuary. A rural remnant of the vast Fraser estuary marshes, the sanctuary is comprised of 344 hectares of managed habitat and estuarine marsh, preserving it as an area of crucial importance to the countless thousands of migratory birds. In all, over 268 species of birds have been sighted at the sanctuary.<br />
<br />
The sanctuary was indeed a really cool birding place with hides and towers. There's also a warming hut where you can rest and eat. We had a lunch break there; Susan and Jim had supplied us with delicious food and beverage. Right next to the hut is a gift shop, with lots of bird related items for sale. Make sure you buy something, to support the sanctuary. In the gift shop we met Brian Self, who joined us later, after he had finished his daily work as one of the bird guides of the Sanctuary.<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpCSBL9zQI/AAAAAAAAAYI/1Q8Q8Qn0hR8/s1600/3_at_Freiser.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpCSBL9zQI/AAAAAAAAAYI/1Q8Q8Qn0hR8/s400/3_at_Freiser.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<span style="font-size: x-small;"><i>Brian, Susan and Jim</i></span> <br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
And what did we see there? Lots of birds, and many species were the same as we have here in Finland. Mallard was the dominant species, there were thousands of them around the area, my best guess is about 2000-3000. All other waterfowl were less numerous, but still there were enough of them to see; some 500 Northern Pintails <i>(Anas acuta)</i>, about 50 Gadwalls <i>(Anas strepera)</i>, 100 American Wigeons, 50 Green-winged Teals <i>(Anas carolinensis)</i>, 10 Northern Shovelers <i>(Anas clypeata)</i>, 5 Wood Ducks, 3 Ring-necked Ducks <i>(Aythya collaris)</i>, 3 Greater Scaups <i>(Aythya marila)</i>, 2 Lesser Scaups <i>(Aythya affinis)</i>, 4 Buffleheads, 50 American Coots, 1 Hooded Merganser <i>(Lophodytes cucullatus)</i>, 1 Ruddy Duck <i>(Oxyura jamaicensis)</i>, 1 Pied-billed Grebe <i>(Podilymbus podiceps)</i> and 1 Horned aka Slavonian Grebe <i>(Podiceps auritus)</i> &#8230; of the previous, the Ring-necked Duck and Lesser Scaup were lifers.<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpDdXieHgI/AAAAAAAAAYQ/uD20qwib2zY/s1600/Aythya+collaris.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpDdXieHgI/AAAAAAAAAYQ/uD20qwib2zY/s320/Aythya+collaris.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<span style="font-size: x-small;"><i>Ring-necked Duck (Aythya collaris) </i></span><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpE-eYEnuI/AAAAAAAAAYY/Ax3eE4cTjMI/s1600/amerikanhaapanoit.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpE-eYEnuI/AAAAAAAAAYY/Ax3eE4cTjMI/s320/amerikanhaapanoit.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<span style="font-size: x-small;"><i>American Wigeons (Anas americana) </i></span><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
Birds of prey were also numerous; 1 lifer i.e. Cooper's Hawk <i>(Accipiter cooperii)</i>, about 10 Red-Tailed Hawks, 1 Rough-legged Buzzards <i>(Buteo lagopus)</i>, some 20 Hen Harriers, 1 Bald Eagle, 1 Merlin <i>(Falco columbarius)</i>, and for my pleasure at least 2 Peregrine Falcons, an adult and juvenile. I really enjoyed watching the Peregrine hunting ducks several times from so close that I could easily see its eyes! And once again I just enjoyed, forgetting my camera&#8230; <br />
<br />
Of other big birds there were some Canada Geese and Snow Geese flying by, 2 Black-crowned Night Herons <i>(Nycticorax nycticorax)</i> were resting in bushes near the gift shop, in total about 5 Great Blue Herons here and there and what's the best: 14 Sandhill Cranes <i>(Grus canadensis)</i> flew over and a bit later they landed by one of the many pools! Very nice!<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: left;"><a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpJpJg7eCI/AAAAAAAAAYg/eG-RPAOaM8w/s1600/cranes.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpJpJg7eCI/AAAAAAAAAYg/eG-RPAOaM8w/s640/cranes.jpg" /></a><span style="font-size: x-small;"><i>Sandhill Cranes (Grus canadensis)</i></span><br />
</div><br />
Waders were scarce. First we saw 1 Wilson's Snipe <i>(Gallinago delicate)</i> and later a flock of 8 Long-billed Dowitchers <i>(Limnodromus scolopaceus)</i> flew by us and later we saw about 28 of them resting in one of the pools. Both species were lifers for me and it was cool to see Dowitchers well.<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpLtIiExII/AAAAAAAAAYo/N-LFszA0JXM/s1600/kurppelot.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpLtIiExII/AAAAAAAAAYo/N-LFszA0JXM/s640/kurppelot.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><span style="font-size: x-small;"><i>Long-billed Dowitchers </i></span><i><span style="font-size: x-small;">(Limnodromus scolopaceus) and Northern Pintails (Anas acuta)</span></i><br />
<i><span style="font-size: x-small;"><br />
</span></i><br />
Smaller birds were not very numerous. About 20 Cedar Waxwings <i>(Bombycilla cedrorum)</i> were nice when they landed in nearby trees. I just wish I'd find one of those from Finland ;-). Northern Flicker showed itself to us fine. Lots of Red-Winged Blackbirds, some Chickadees, House Sparrows, Feral Pigeons but not much more.<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swp4-NWn0vI/AAAAAAAAAaA/8jYkog177Q0/s1600/ruokinta.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swp4-NWn0vI/AAAAAAAAAaA/8jYkog177Q0/s320/ruokinta.jpg" yr="true" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<i><span style="font-size: x-small;">Red-Winged Blackbirds (Agelaius phoeniceus)</span></i><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
To sum up the visit to Reifel: A must see place, I got 6 lifers there and several unforgettable experiences! If I ever come back to BC, I&#8217;ll definitely visit Reifel again.<br />
<br />
I already thought that I had seen everything this day, but how wrong I was&#8230; Brian took the position of the main guide when we jumped back in our cars and drove further south, but not far. Our last target was Boundary Bay. I expected some waders, well, ok, there were really &#8220;some&#8221;. Amazing place, Vancouver birders are really spoiled! In front of me were 30 000 Dunlins <i>(Calidris alpina)</i>, some 5000 waterfowl, mainly American Wigeons and Northern Pintails, also Mallards, Green-winged Teals, Scaups and what else&#8230;? How to find birds from these masses? Especially when a Merlin attacks the Dunlins and all 30 000 waders get in to flight&#8230; In addition to that it was already late afternoon, sun was about to set, dark clouds covered the sky and it started to rain again&#8230;<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpPdq0cmEI/AAAAAAAAAYw/B4IssCUJCic/s1600/1_At_Boundary_Bay.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpPdq0cmEI/AAAAAAAAAYw/B4IssCUJCic/s320/1_At_Boundary_Bay.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<span style="font-size: x-small;"><i>Jim, Susan &amp; Brian at Boundary Bay </i></span><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
But Brian did it. He picked from the wader mass first 8 Black-bellied Plovers <i>(Pluvialis squatarola)</i>, a bit later 8 Western Sandpipers <i>(Calidris mauri)</i>, and finally 2 Greater Yellowlegs <i>(Tringa melanoleuca)</i>. Last two were welcome lifers for me!<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpQvCioJAI/AAAAAAAAAY4/z9KBf5I2tk8/s1600/amerikanviklo.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpQvCioJAI/AAAAAAAAAY4/z9KBf5I2tk8/s320/amerikanviklo.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
&nbsp;<span style="font-size: x-small;"><i>Greater Yellowleg (Tringa melanoleuca)</i></span><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
Some information about Boundary Bay: it is a major stop-over on the Pacific Flyway, in the spring and fall, it attracts 50 different species of shorebirds numbering in the hundreds of thousands. Travelling between wintering grounds in South or Central America and nesting areas north in the high Arctic, shorebirds, waterfowl and songbirds stop to rest and feed. So far 333 species have been recorded; it supports more than 100 000 wintering waterfowl, the last Canadian nesting population of Barn Owls and one of Canada's largest winter populations of raptors, with 22 recorded species.<br />
<br />
My visit to Boundary Bay was too short, but I&#8217;m more than happy that I could see at least a bit of it! <br />
The day was perfect &#8211; thanks to Susan, Jim and Brian!<br />
<br />
Day 3. 31/10/2009<br />
<br />
To get fast out of downtown, I took the SkyTrain from Waterfront to Bridgeport, where I met Brian. SkyTrain is a bit misleading name, because more than half of the travel time, the train was underground. Nevertheless, it&#8217;s the best way to get out of town. I saw one more Bald Eagle from the train, near Bridgeport.<br />
<br />
Our first target was somewhere around Delta, a green field where we were supposed to find Trumpeter Swans, but perhaps it was still too early in the morning, as they were not there. Not a good start, but rest of the morning made me forget this.<br />
<br />
The main target this morning was Point Roberts, which is a cape to the south of Vancouver, pointing south, about 1 hour drive from Vancouver. Funny thing is that it&#8217;s a kind of isolated part of the USA, so there is naturally normal border bureaucracy when you go there. When we got rid of border control and were ready to jump into the car, I heard something that sounded like a woodpecker to me. It was somewhere in the forest, but fortunately it came out and we could see it well; a Hairy Woodpecker <i>(Picoides villosus)</i>. Nice! And the first lifer for me in Point Roberts.<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpUB1tmtZI/AAAAAAAAAZQ/jSaUCuLkmEs/s1600/eagles.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpUB1tmtZI/AAAAAAAAAZQ/jSaUCuLkmEs/s320/eagles.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<i><span style="font-size: x-small;">Bald Eagles (Haliaeetus leucocephalus) at Point Roberts</span></i><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
Brian drove straight to the shore, to Lighthouse Marine Park. I saw immediately two adult Bald Eagles on the pier; the other was eating a big fish. After that we concentrated on the sea. The sky was almost clear, sun was shining, but the wind was too strong. Big waves on the sea, it was hard to find anything from there. I was hoping for Alcids, especially Murrelets, Auklets or Puffins. After thorough search I found a flock of some Alcids. Both Brian and I stared at them through Brian&#8217;s scope, and the identification was clear: Common Murres <i>(Uria aalge)</i>. Oh well... Alcids, but not what I hoped, this species breeds in Finland too. We continued the search and then I found another Alcid. Hmmm, looks different, yes, it was a Pigeon Guillemot <i>(Cepphus columba)</i>. Lifer! Cool! But that&#8217;s all the Alcids this day; the sea was so rough that it was impossible to find birds that were far from shore. Closer to shore there were some waterfowl, like Oldsquaws i.e. Long-tailed Ducks <i>(Clangula hyemalis)</i>, Goosander aka Common Merganser <i>(Mergus merganser)</i>, Red-breasted Mergansers <i>(Mergus serrator)</i>, Surf Scoters, White-Winged Scoters, some Horned Grebes i.e. Slavonian Grebes <i>(Podiceps auritus)</i>, some Red-necked Grebes <i>(Podiceps grisegena)</i>, Double-crested Cormorants and at least 1 Brandt&#8217;s Cormorant <i>(Phalarocorax penicillatus)</i>, which was a lifer for me. Further east there were more waterfowl close to the shore and there it was, one of the species that I had hoped to see; Harlequin Duck <i>(Histrionicus histrionicus)</i> and not just one or two, but about 30 of them. Perfect! Oh, and almost forgot to tell about the Loons; Red-throated Loons <i>(Gavia stellata)</i> were the most numerous, I saw at least 6 or 7 of them, Common Loon was not so common, only 2 of them were in vicinity. For me the most interesting was naturally a Loon that flew by; it looked like Arctic Loon, but in this area it was naturally the Pacific Loon <i>(Gavia pacifica)</i> &#8211; lifer for me!<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpVMmpf35I/AAAAAAAAAZY/VNAGxXePMRw/s1600/2_Harlequins.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpVMmpf35I/AAAAAAAAAZY/VNAGxXePMRw/s320/2_Harlequins.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<span style="font-size: x-small;"><i>Harlequin Ducks (Histrionicus histrionicus)</i></span> <br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
Behind the shore wall there were thick bushes, with small openings between them. It was time to concentrate on passerines. For me this meant again new lifers; White-crowned Sparrows <i>(Zonotrichia leucophrys)</i>, Golden-crowned Sparrows <i>(Zonotrichia atricapilla)</i> and House Finches <i>(Carpodacus mexicanus)</i>. In addition to those there were also Song Sparrows, Fox Sparrows and Dark-eyed Juncos, but those I had already met in Stanley Park. All these Sparrows were in mixed flocks so it was easy to compare and learn to identify them. While watching these Sparrows I saw yet another Sparrow, which was completely different than others. I saw it in good lighting only some 5-10 seconds but from very close distance, it was only some 5 meters away. It was much paler than other Sparrows in same flock, tail looked longer and had white outer tail feathers. It was streaked all over, except belly was whitish. And it had a white eye-ring. Unfortunately Brian did not see it but he supposed that it was a Vesper Sparrow <i>(Pooecetes gramineus)</i>, uncommon species in this area in this time of year. I checked Sibley and the only possibility indeed is the Vesper Sparrow! Not bad!<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpW108AsHI/AAAAAAAAAZg/AnSdRmF1dgU/s1600/white-crowned.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpW108AsHI/AAAAAAAAAZg/AnSdRmF1dgU/s320/white-crowned.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<i><span style="font-size: x-small;">White-crowned Sparrow (Zonotrichia leucophrys), female</span></i><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpX591xNBI/AAAAAAAAAZo/m1GJ6cvoYwM/s1600/house+finch.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/SwpX591xNBI/AAAAAAAAAZo/m1GJ6cvoYwM/s320/house+finch.jpg" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<span style="font-size: x-small;"><i>House Finch </i><i>(Carpodacus mexicanus), male</i></span> <br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
From bushes we moved to the nearby village and Point Roberts Marina. There was a small pond, where there were 3 Buffleheads, 3 Hooded Mergansers plus about 10 American Wigeons. Brian drove to other side of the pond where there is a small field. We were tromping around a few minutes and there it was, the Wilson&#8217;s Snipe. This time I saw it much better than in Reifel. Nice!<br />
<br />
What else did we see in Point Roberts? A flock of 20 Cedar Waxwings, about 7 Nothern Flickers, Eurasian Starlings, 1 Merlin, 1 Northern Harrier and 1 American Black Oystercatcher. We tried to find also Belted Kingfisher and Black Turnstone from the marina, but no luck with them.<br />
<br />
And while driving back to Canada and Vancouver, I saw again several Northern Harriers, couple of Red-tailed Hawks and one Bald Eagle.<br />
<br />
Very good morning, thanks Brian!<br />
<br />
Days 4.-6. 1&#8211;3/11/2009 <br />
<br />
Not much to see around Vancouver Convention Centre on the Waterfront. Glaucous-winged Gulls, Northwestern Crows, some Eurasian Starlings. Only highlight was the Bald Eagle flying past the hotel. In the late afternoon of 3rd, I was waiting for the Amtrak Thruway coach at Pacific Central Station. Continuous flow of&nbsp; Northwestern Crows, totally hundreds of them, came from west and they disappeared somewhere to east, perhaps to Burnaby? This continued at least 30 minutes i.e. the whole time that I spent outside the station building. Two flocks of Canada Geese, about 30 birds, landed in the empty lot between National Avenue and Prior Street. Also lots of big gulls, probably Glaucous-winged, were flying around and resting on the station roof.<br />
<br />
<div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"><a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swp-ucMYKrI/AAAAAAAAAaI/gL-4qQW9tcU/s1600/varikset.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"><img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_XHVt2dYjg7w/Swp-ucMYKrI/AAAAAAAAAaI/gL-4qQW9tcU/s320/varikset.jpg" yr="true" /></a><br />
</div><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
<span style="font-size: x-small;"><i>Continuous flow of Northwestern Crows (Corvus caurinus) on their road to somewhere east</i></span><br />
<br />
<br />
<br />
I was hoping to see some owl during evening travel to Seattle, but no luck this time. But all in all, this was a&nbsp;great birding experience.<br />
<br />
<b>More information:</b><br />
<br />
Stanley Park: <a href="http://vancouver.ca/PARKS/parks/Stanley/">Welcome to Stanley Park</a><br />
<br />
Reifel: <a href="http://www.reifelbirdsanctuary.com/">George C. Reifel Migratory Bird Sanctuary</a> <br />
<br />
Boundary Bay: <a href="http://www.birdsonthebay.ca/">Birds on the Bay</a><br />
<br />
Other&nbsp;locations: <a href="http://www.naturevancouver.ca/Birding_Birding_Sites">Birding Spots in Metro Vancouver Area</a>

  <tr>
    <td width="627" valign="top" align="center" bordercolorlight="#0000FF">
      <!--mstheme--><font face="Times New Roman, Times">
      <p align="justify"><b><font color="#000080" size="5">About Us&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
      </font></b><applet code="fphover.class" codebase="./" width="83" height="29">
  <param name="color" value="#000080">
  <param name="hovercolor" value="#0000FF">
  <param name="textcolor" value="#FFFFCC">
  <param name="effect" value="glow">
  <param name="font" value="Dialog">
  <param name="fontstyle" value="bold">
  <param name="fontsize" value="14">
  <param name="url" valuetype="ref" value="contact.htm">
  <param name="text" value="CONTACT">
</applet>
      </p>
      <p align="justify"><a href="llanf.jpg"><img border="2" src="grp_lnf.jpg" alt="Liverpool RSPB at Conwy" width="286" height="125"></a></p>
      <p align="justify"><font size="4">The Liverpool RSPB Local Group was
      formed in 1972 to support the work of the RSPB in protecting birds and
      their environment. Liverpool is centrally placed in a string of coastal
      sites and reserves in North West England and North Wales from Leighton
      Moss and Morecambe Bay in the north to Glan Conwy in the west .&nbsp;<br>
      </font></p>
      <p align="justify"><font size="4">Not far away the upland areas of the
      Pennines, Snowdonia, and the Lake District are easily reached. Along the
      coast to the north of the city a line of local and national nature
      reserves take the birdwatcher to the River Ribble estuary while to the
      south lie the&nbsp; estuaries of the&nbsp; Mersey and Dee which are of
      international importance for wintering wildfowl and waders.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<br>
      <br>
      A program of slide and film presentations covering a&nbsp; cross section
      of wildlife interests are held during the year and frequent and varied,
      outdoor trips are arranged to sites of interest,&nbsp; throughout the
      North West of England&nbsp; and North Wales.&nbsp;</font></p>
      <p align="justify"><font size="4">Eight indoor meetings are held during
      the year with a four month break during the Summer months. Held on the
      third Monday of the month, in the church hall of Mossley Hill Parish
      Church, ( St. Mathew and St. James ) at the junction of Rose Lane and
      Mossley Hill road. Meetings commence at 7.30pm and last about 2 hours with
      an interval for refreshments.&nbsp;&nbsp;Admission charges apply.&nbsp;</font></p>
      <p align="center"><a href="Sparrows.htm"><img border="2" src="Eh%20amigo.jpg" alt="The mexican!  - female Sparrow with mealworm" width="162" height="154"></a>
      <p align="center">&nbsp;<font size="4"> Eh Amigo! sure makes a change from bread!&nbsp;</font></p>

      <p align="justify"><b><font color="#000080" size="4">Anniversary</font><font color="#000080" size="5">&nbsp;</font></b></p>
      <p align="justify"><font size="4">2002 marked the 30th anniversary of the
      Liverpool Group making it one of the oldest members groups in the U.K.
      Through membership of the group we help the RSPB in its work by making
      financial donations and by taking part in surveys and educational
      projects.&nbsp; With your help this essential work can be increased, if
      you would like to join with us then please apply by clicking on the link
      below.</font></p>

      if(window.addEventListener) {

<div class='post'>
<h1 class='title'><a href='http://christysnaturestuff.blogspot.com/2009/03/snow-day.html'>Snow Day!!</a></h1>
     Published by Christy under 
         
<a href='http://christysnaturestuff.blogspot.com/search/label/birds' rel='tag'>birds</a>,
<a href='http://christysnaturestuff.blogspot.com/search/label/march%203%20snow%20storm' rel='tag'>march 3 snow storm</a>,
<a href='http://christysnaturestuff.blogspot.com/search/label/north%20carolina%20snow' rel='tag'>north carolina snow</a>,
<a href='http://christysnaturestuff.blogspot.com/search/label/snow' rel='tag'>snow</a>,
<a href='http://christysnaturestuff.blogspot.com/search/label/snow%20fun' rel='tag'>snow fun</a>,
<a href='http://christysnaturestuff.blogspot.com/search/label/winter' rel='tag'>winter</a>
 on 2:27 PM
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
Now it's common knowledge that we just don't get a lot of the white stuff down here in the south, but Sunday night it snowed and we got a pretty fair amount! 6 to 12 inches in some spots. It was GREAT! I think I spent the majority of yesterday out with the camera, the scene was just so beautiful. I took almost 700 photos! Of course, after weeding through them the 'ok' shots were numbered WAY below 700. :)<br /><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a onblur="try {parent.deselectBloggerImageGracefully();} catch(e) {}" href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_dBzjmuwC0oA/Sa2Ud3bHqHI/AAAAAAAABpU/7kqdysYVeK8/s1600-h/P3021292.JPG"><img style="margin: 0px auto 10px; display: block; text-align: center; cursor: pointer; width: 320px; height: 257px;" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_dBzjmuwC0oA/Sa2Ud3bHqHI/AAAAAAAABpU/7kqdysYVeK8/s320/P3021292.JPG" alt="" id="BLOGGER_PHOTO_ID_5309062776554498162" border="0" /></a><br />These guys were out sledding around with a four wheeler and their dog! I think the dog was even enjoying it.<br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><a onblur="try {parent.deselectBloggerImageGracefully();} catch(e) {}" href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_dBzjmuwC0oA/Sa2Udgyvd5I/AAAAAAAABpM/y8NBSc3UuZ8/s1600-h/P3021291.JPG"><img style="margin: 0px auto 10px; display: block; text-align: center; cursor: pointer; width: 320px; height: 150px;" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/_dBzjmuwC0oA/Sa2Udgyvd5I/AAAAAAAABpM/y8NBSc3UuZ8/s320/P3021291.JPG" alt="" id="BLOGGER_PHOTO_ID_5309062770479560594" border="0" /></a><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><br />This lone grackle showed up at one of the feeding stations yesterday for some suet. It was so funny to watch. As the snow would fall from the trees, the grackle seemed to be opening it's mouth specifically to catch the flakes as they drifted by.<br /></div><a onblur="try {parent.deselectBloggerImageGracefully();} catch(e) {}" href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_dBzjmuwC0oA/Sa2UdFo2lyI/AAAAAAAABo8/fAHwNlQFSoE/s1600-h/P3021200.JPG"><img style="margin: 0px auto 10px; display: block; text-align: center; cursor: pointer; width: 245px; height: 320px;" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_dBzjmuwC0oA/Sa2UdFo2lyI/AAAAAAAABo8/fAHwNlQFSoE/s320/P3021200.JPG" alt="" id="BLOGGER_PHOTO_ID_5309062763190327074" border="0" /></a><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a onblur="try {parent.deselectBloggerImageGracefully();} catch(e) {}" href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_dBzjmuwC0oA/Sa2Ucey5-zI/AAAAAAAABo0/CV-dIHjwfzU/s1600-h/P3021197.JPG"><img style="margin: 0px auto 10px; <h1 class='title'><a href='http://christysnaturestuff.blogspot.com/2009/02/bluebird.html'>Bluebird</a></h1>
     Published by Christy under 
         
<a href='http://christysnaturestuff.blogspot.com/search/label/birding' rel='tag'>birding</a>,
<a href='http://christysnaturestuff.blogspot.com/search/label/bluebird' rel='tag'>bluebird</a>,
<a href='http://christysnaturestuff.blogspot.com/search/label/eastern%20bluebird' rel='tag'>eastern bluebird</a>,
<a href='http://christysnaturestuff.blogspot.com/search/label/North%20Carolina%20birds' rel='tag'>North Carolina birds</a>,
<a href='http://christysnaturestuff.blogspot.com/search/label/wild%20birds' rel='tag'>wild birds</a>
 on 2:47 PM
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
Bluebirds are just cute. This fellow was sitting on top of an old okra stalk with one of his buddies...till I grabbed the camera of course. I did manage to get a few shots of one of them though. They are pretty severely cropped, hence the size, but he's still just the cutest thing!<a onblur="try {parent.deselectBloggerImageGracefully();} catch(e) {}" href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/_dBzjmuwC0oA/SaWhCcG6_DI/AAAAAAAABnc/iu8YxZa3yhI/s1600-h/P2240586.JPG"><img style="display:block;       if(window.addEventListener) {

<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='6385901639769571729'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://midmarsh.blogspot.com/2010/05/minibeasts.html'>Minibeasts</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
When I was primary school teaching one of the things my class and I looked forward to was a day out of the classroom exploring the local countryside to see what insects and other small creatures were about. Then would come the task of identification and finding out about their life cycle and habitat.That was in the days before the National Curriculum when the teacher was able to decide what the children would learn, chose the best times to go about it and allotted the time needed accordingly.<br />
<br />
Not too many minibeasts spotted here at the moment. At last I did find one Speckled Wood Butterfly taking a rest from flitting all over the place though it steadfastly refused to open its wings fully.<br />
<br />
<a href="http://www.flickr.com/photos/m1btr/4598441375/" title="Speckled Wood by Midmarsh John, on Flickr"><img src="http://farm2.static.flickr.com/1340/4598441375_8596976bb9.jpg" width="500" height="267" alt="Speckled Wood" /></a><br />
<br />
I am not sure whether this was a small beetle or a small fly which spent a couple of hours on the outside of the kitchen window. It made a change to get a blue sky as a background!<br />
<br />
<a href="http://www.flickr.com/photos/m1btr/4598376381/" title="Small Fly by Midmarsh John, on Flickr"><img src="http://farm5.static.flickr.com/4026/4598376381_96abe3d241.jpg" width="500" height="277" alt="Small Fly" /></a><br />
<br />
A Hoverfly taking a rare rest.<br />
<br />
<a href="http://www.flickr.com/photos/m1btr/4598995052/" title="Hoverfly by Midmarsh John, on Flickr"><img src="http://farm2.static.flickr.com/1377/4598995052_3bb1fcd02a.jpg" width="500" height="277" alt="Hoverfly" /></a><br />
<br />
Here a Common Green Shield Bug which spent about six hours in the same position on the outside of the kitchen window. The only time it moved was to angle itself to catch the Sun when it eventually lit up that part of the window,<br />
<br />
<a href="http://www.flickr.com/photos/m1btr/4598996534/" title="Common Green Shield Bug by Midmarsh John, on Flickr"><img src="http://farm2.static.flickr.com/1099/4598996534_2e0792cb09.jpg" width="500" height="402" alt="Common Green Shield Bug" /></a><br />
<br />
It wasn't until I enlarged the photos that I noticed that this little bug was missing two of its six legs.<br />
<br />
<a href="http://www.flickr.com/photos/m1btr/4598376881/" title="Common Green Shield Bug by Midmarsh John, on Flickr"><img src="http://farm5.static.flickr.com/4018/4598376881_79224f8bb7.jpg" width="500" height="389" alt="Common Green Shield Bug" /></a><br />
<br />
All the above were taken with the Lumix TZ7. There are still scores of seven spot ladybirds about. The tadpoles in the nursery pond are coming along nicely.
<div style='clear: both;'></div>
</div>

<h2 class='date-header'><span>Tuesday, May 11, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='5327415045195656479'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://midmarsh.blogspot.com/2010/05/hedgehogs-inside-and-out.html'>Hedgehogs Inside and Out</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
Inside their own house that is. One youngster, having eaten earlier, decided to have a nap in the Hedgehog House. At the same time another juvenile arrived for a meal and a drink of water. Later on it was joined by an adult. All was good tempered last night with no sign of them playing at being bulldozers.<br />
<br />
<object width="500" height="405"><param name="movie" value="http://www.youtube.com/v/yKSyYoVV7IY&hl=en_GB&fs=1&rel=0&color1=0x3a3a3a&color2=0x999999&border=1"></param><param name="allowFullScreen" value="true"></param><param name="allowscriptaccess" value="always"></param><embed src="http://www.youtube.com/v/yKSyYoVV7IY&hl=en_GB&fs=1&rel=0&color1=0x3a3a3a&color2=0x999999&border=1" type="application/x-shockwave-flash" allowscriptaccess="always" allowfullscreen="true" width="500" height="405"></embed></object><br />
<br />
What a kerfuffal trying to upload the video. Twice to Flickr only to be told each time that it couldn't be processed. Then tried YouTube and all worked first time. Action will be about double speed as the recorder drops to 12.5 fps when recording two feeds at the same time.

<h2 class='date-header'><span>Monday, May 10, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='3655233166850970950'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://midmarsh.blogspot.com/2010/05/bit-of-puzzle-what-did-i-see.html'>A bit of a Puzzle. What Did I See?</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
Unfortunately no photo, not even a fuzzy one. As we were walking through the churchyard this afternoon we came face to face with an animal. Unfortunately as soon as it spotted us it vanished before I could get the camera out of my pocket.<br />
<br />
At first I thought - ah a Muntjac as it reminded me of some of the pictures I have seen. Looking up their description they appear to be not much bigger than Bobby but this seemed quite a bit larger. Colour brown with no markings. The puzzle was it appeared to have horns rather than antlers. Two unbranched horns about a foot in length, vertical and curving backwards slightly. Maybe it was a goat but it wasn't like any I have seen in the past. No sign of a beard either.<br />
<br />
We went for a wander in the couple of the vdirections it could have gone but there was no sign of it and, unusually, Bobby didn't seem to pick up a scent.
<div style='clear: both;'></div>

<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://midmarsh.blogspot.com/2010/05/hedgehog-intruder.html'>Hedgehog Intruder</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
Two Hedgehogs into One Hedgehog House doesn't go. One juvenile had made a few brief visits to their house and on one occasion another decided to enter. The intruder tries a little shoving but ends up being pushed back to the entrance.<br />
<br />
<object width="500" height="405"><param name="movie" value="http://www.youtube.com/v/dzmsoBhWgcY&hl=en_GB&fs=1&rel=0&color1=0x3a3a3a&color2=0x999999&border=1"></param><param name="allowFullScreen" value="true"></param><param name="allowscriptaccess" value="always"></param><embed src="http://www.youtube.com/v/dzmsoBhWgcY&hl=en_GB&fs=1&rel=0&color1=0x3a3a3a&color2=0x999999&border=1" type="application/x-shockwave-flash" allowscriptaccess="always" allowfullscreen="true" width="500" height="405"></embed></object><br />
<br />
Later on one juvenile was seen feeding but no hedgehog was in the house this morning.

<h2 class='date-header'><span>Sunday, May 9, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='3468918369115353391'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://midmarsh.blogspot.com/2010/05/raptor-comes-visiting-but-leaves-empty.html'>A Raptor Comes Visiting but Leaves Empty Taloned</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
I have waited a long time to get a photograph of one of the visiting raptors which frequent the garden from time to time. When I went to the kitchen to make a cuppa I had a quick glance round the garden as I always do. Down the end of the garden partly hidden behind the greenhouse was a Female Sparrowhawk perched on an old wooden frame. At least I hope it is a Female Sparrowhawk.<br />
<br />
<a href="http://www.flickr.com/photos/m1btr/4590649759/" title="Female Sparrowhawk? by Midmarsh John, on Flickr"><img src="http://farm5.static.flickr.com/4008/4590649759_7e02fe6719.jpg" width="500" height="375" alt="Female Sparrowhawk?" /></a><br />
<br />
Not the sharpest of photos but at least I got some shots this time. So as not to scare her away I stayed as far back in the kitchen as I could. I think the sight of a wrinkly pointing a 400mm zoom lens right next to the window would have made her depart rapidly.<br />
<br />
<a href="http://www.flickr.com/photos/m1btr/4591269696/" title="Female Sparrowhawk? by Midmarsh John, on Flickr"><img alt="Female Sparrowhawk?" height="375" src="http://farm5.static.flickr.com/4062/4591269696_b22e676290.jpg" width="500" /></a><br />
<br />
She stayed for a short while looking at all the places she knew there should be small birds feeding but as can be expected there was not a sight or sound of the usual visitors.<br />
<br />
<a href="http://www.flickr.com/photos/m1btr/4590650167/" title="Female Sparrowhawk? by Midmarsh John, on Flickr"><img alt="Female Sparrowhawk?" height="372" src="http://farm5.static.flickr.com/4005/4590650167_044e646d1e.jpg" width="500" /></a><br />
<br />
It seemed to be unusual to see a hunting bird perched less than 6 feet (2m) from the ground. I managed to get half a dozen shots before she took off for pastures new. The one thing which surprised me was how small she was. Somehow I had expected a bird of prey to be larger. To give an idea of her size the horizontal piece of wood in the background is about two inches (24mm) high.<br />
<br />
If you are interested and haven't had a look yet at the Peregrine Falcon chicks on Derby Cathedral I think their site is worth a visit. They are very recently hatched and the video of the parent bird being so gentle while feeding the chicks is a sight for sore eyes. It's not too gory and can be seen <b><a href="http://derbyperegrines.blogspot.com/2010/05/picture-perfect.html">HERE</a></b>.<br />
<br />
<br />
I was only wondering yesterday whether the local Heron had survived the weeks of frozen water in the past winter. Well, I did see one this morning weaving round the sky being hotly pursued by three Rooks.
<div style='clear: both;'></div>
</div>

        <div class="date-outer">
      
<h2 class='date-header'><span>Saturday, May 8, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='7391677330315906403'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://midmarsh.blogspot.com/2010/05/hedgehog-sumo-wrestling.html'>Hedgehog Sumo Wrestling</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
The youngster who had nested in the Hedgehog House left just before 8 p.m. and it, or the other youngster, arrived back about 10.30 p.m. for its first free meal.<br />
<br />
These were taken with the 350D. The rubber lens hood I use has the ability to distort and give a reasonable seal on the glass of the kitchen window. This keeps some of the flash away from the lens but there is some reflection from the outer panel in the double glazing. The second shot was taken through the single glazed door of the conservatory. <br />
<br />
<a href="http://www.flickr.com/photos/m1btr/4588408679/" title="Hedgehog by Midmarsh John, on Flickr"><img alt="Hedgehog" height="277" src="http://farm5.static.flickr.com/4044/4588408679_4fa23a38d5.jpg" width="500" /></a><br />
<br />
<a href="http://www.flickr.com/photos/m1btr/4588409081/" title="Hedgehog by Midmarsh John, on Flickr"><img alt="Hedgehog" height="227" src="http://farm5.static.flickr.com/4043/4588409081_3428952152_m.jpg" width="240" /></a><br />
<br />
It was gently raining at the time and by the looks of it it had rained most of the night so I wasn't expecting much hog activity. This afternoon I decided to look through the recordings and found one hog had been in their house for a short while soon after midnight.<br />
<br />
What caught my attention though was this group. The way they were charging and pushing each other out of the way reminded me of Sumo wrestlers. Maybe I should paint a white circle so we can see who is the winner. I can't work out whether there are three or four hogs at this time but I am fairly certain that two of them were adults.<br />
<br />
<object height="405" width="500"><param name="movie" value="http://www.youtube.com/v/ZIF4_bp958Q&amp;hl=en_GB&amp;fs=1&amp;rel=0&amp;color1=0x2b405b&amp;color2=0x6b8ab6&amp;border=1"></param><param name="allowFullScreen" value="true"></param><param name="allowscriptaccess" value="always"></param><embed src="http://www.youtube.com/v/ZIF4_bp958Q&amp;hl=en_GB&amp;fs=1&amp;rel=0&amp;color1=0x2b405b&amp;color2=0x6b8ab6&amp;border=1" type="application/x-shockwave-flash" allowscriptaccess="always" allowfullscreen="true" width="500" height="405"></embed></object><br />
<br />
In spite of all the argy bargy the young Hedgehog returned some while later and managed to get a quiet meal. No takers for the Hedgehog House today though.
<div style='clear: both;'></div>

<a class='comment-link' href='http://midmarsh.blogspot.com/2010/05/hedgehog-sumo-wrestling.html#comments' onclick=''>10
comments. All are welcome.</a>
</span>

          </div></div>
        

        <div class="date-outer">
      
<h2 class='date-header'><span>Friday, May 7, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post hentry'>
<a name='5142066151730542978'></a>
<h3 class='post-title entry-title'>
<a href='http://midmarsh.blogspot.com/2010/05/friday-at-flicks-im-hungry.html'>Friday at the Flicks  -  I'm Hungry + Hedgehog Meets Magpie</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header'>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
</div>
<div class='post-body entry-content'>
As far as I can see the two young Hedgehogs visit every night and really enjoy their food. In fact they eat as though they hadn't eaten for a fortnight.&nbsp; This clip was taken with the Hitachi DVD camcorder set to night vision so movement will be a bit blurred.<br />
<br />
<object height="405" width="500"><param name="movie" value="http://www.youtube.com/v/oWhR8BJjnLA&amp;hl=en_GB&amp;fs=1&amp;rel=0&amp;color1=0x2b405b&amp;color2=0x6b8ab6&amp;border=1"></param><param name="allowFullScreen" value="true"></param><param name="allowscriptaccess" value="always"></param><embed src="http://www.youtube.com/v/oWhR8BJjnLA&amp;hl=en_GB&amp;fs=1&amp;rel=0&amp;color1=0x2b405b&amp;color2=0x6b8ab6&amp;border=1" type="application/x-shockwave-flash" allowscriptaccess="always" allowfullscreen="true" width="500" height="405"></embed></object><br />
<br />
In the same food dish there are also seeds for the early morning birds. The first to arrive at the moment is a Magpie closely followed by a group of Rooks all trying to fill their throat pouches as fast as they can. At this time when they are probably feeding their young their usual sociable nature becomes a bit strained. It was a pity there is no microphone with that camera so you will have to imagine the squawking and cawing going on.<br />
<br />
<object height="405" width="500"><param name="movie" value="http://www.youtube.com/v/eaEaIToqgu4&amp;hl=en_GB&amp;fs=1&amp;rel=0&amp;color1=0x2b405b&amp;color2=0x6b8ab6&amp;border=1"></param><param name="allowFullScreen" value="true"></param><param name="allowscriptaccess" value="always"></param><embed src="http://www.youtube.com/v/eaEaIToqgu4&amp;hl=en_GB&amp;fs=1&amp;rel=0&amp;color1=0x2b405b&amp;color2=0x6b8ab6&amp;border=1" type="application/x-shockwave-flash" allowscriptaccess="always" allowfullscreen="true" width="500" height="405"></embed></object><br />
<br />
I was pleased to see that there is a Hedgehog once again sleeping in the Hedgehog House, the first time for three days. I had a look to see at what time the hog entered the box and found this - The Hedgehog arrived for a late night snack and at the same time a Magpie arrived for its early morning feed. A minute or so later the hog made its bed in the house.<br />
<br />
<object height="405" width="500"><param name="movie" value="http://www.youtube.com/v/YDX5UYjp2Hc&amp;hl=en_GB&amp;fs=1&amp;rel=0&amp;color1=0x2b405b&amp;color2=0x6b8ab6&amp;border=1"></param><param name="allowFullScreen" value="true"></param><param name="allowscriptaccess" value="always"></param><embed src="http://www.youtube.com/v/YDX5UYjp2Hc&amp;hl=en_GB&amp;fs=1&amp;rel=0&amp;color1=0x2b405b&amp;color2=0x6b8ab6&amp;border=1" type="application/x-shockwave-flash" allowscriptaccess="always" allowfullscreen="true" width="500" height="405"></embed></object><br />
<br />
Have a great weekend observing the wildlife around you. You never know what is going to happen next.<br />
By the way. I have just heard my first Cuckoo, not just of this Spring but the first I have heard in a few years.
<div style='clear: both;'></div>
</div>

<h2 class='title'>MIDMARSH JOTTINGS</h2>
<div class='widget-content'>
<span style="color: rgb(0, 0, 153); font-weight: bold;">A mainly pictorial record of the wildlife and events in my part of Lincolnshire. My garden has two bird nest boxes and a Hedgehog house fitted with video cameras. There are several bird feeders and a large pond attracting a variety of wildlife to the garden.</span><span style="color: rgb(0, 0, 153); font-weight: bold;"><br /><br />Most of the pictures in the main part of my Jottings can be clicked on for larger views.<br /><br /></span><span style="color: rgb(0, 0, 153);">Many of my video clips are stored on my <a style="font-style: italic;" href="http://www.youtube.com/midmarsh#grid/all">YouYube</a> account<span style="font-weight: bold;">. </span></span><span style="color: rgb(0, 0, 153); font-weight: bold;"><br /><br />Thank you for visiting and remember you are always welcome to leave any comments.  John.<br /><br /></span>
</div>

<h2>About Me</h2>
<div class='widget-content'>
<a href='http://www.blogger.com/profile/10980417659811194899'><img alt='My Photo' class='profile-img' height='80' src='http://2.bp.blogspot.com/_uqHok7S7TXw/SrXx4iVUsrI/AAAAAAAABnc/tjPFr2_xcZI/S220/Me+2a.jpg' width='57'/></a>
<dl class='profile-datablock'>
<dt class='profile-data'>Midmarsh John</dt>
<dd class='profile-data'>Lincolnshire, United Kingdom</dd>
<dd class='profile-textblock'>Yes - that is a photo of me taken about 60 years ago. For many years I always had my trusty Ricoh XRX SLR with me wherever I went. One day the mirror jammed and repair costs were beyond my reach at that time so that was the end of that. 
Now I am retired and have invested in Canon equipment I am able to enjoy a photographic hobby again. These days I concentrate mainly on wildlife and occasional events near to home. 
My interest and use of computers extends back to the days of the ZX81 and BBC computer. For many years I used an Atari ST.</dd>
</dl>

<script type="text/javascript">
      if (!window.registeredBloggerCallbacks) {
        window.registeredBloggerCallbacks = true;

        

        
        gadgets.rpc.register('requestReload', function() {
          document.location.reload();
        });

        
        gadgets.rpc.register('requestSignOut', function(siteId) {
          
          google.friendconnect.container.openSocialSiteId = siteId;
          google.friendconnect.requestSignOut();
        });
      }
    </script>
<script type="text/javascript">
    
    function registerGetBlogUrls() {
      gadgets.rpc.register('getBlogUrls', function() {
        var holder = {};
        
          
          
          
            holder.postFeed = "http://www.blogger.com/feeds/8626959970978663576/posts/default";
          
          
          
            holder.commentFeed = "http://www.blogger.com/feeds/8626959970978663576/comments/default";
          
          holder.currentBlogUrl = "http://midmarsh.blogspot.com/";
          holder.currentBlogId = "8626959970978663576";
        
        return holder;
      });
    }
  </script>

  if (!window.registeredCommonBloggerCallbacks) {

    gadgets.rpc.register('resize_iframe', function(height) {
      var el = document.getElementById(this['f']);
      if (el) {
        el.style.height = height + 'px';
      }
    });

    
    gadgets.rpc.register('set_pref', function() {});

    registerGetBlogUrls();
  }
  </script>

<div style="position:absolute; left:1px; top:179px; width:621px; height:30px;">
    <p class="Index-Title-P"><span class="Index-Title-C">Welcome to the website of the RSPB Medway Local Group</span></p>
</div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:12px; top:236px; width:420px; height:798px;">
    <p class="Normal-P"><span class="Normal-C">The group was formed in 1974 (over 35 years ago and still going strong) and was one of the earliest groups to be set up by the RSPB in the country with the aim of helping to further the work of the RSPB at a local level. <br></span></p>
    <p class="Normal-P"><span class="Normal-C">To that end we organise a programme of both indoor and outdoor meetings, assist on local reserves with work groups, attend local events selling RSPB goods and birdfood, and showing what a wealth of wildlife we have in North Kent.<br></span></p>
    <p class="Normal-P"><span class="Normal-C">We concentrate on the areas of North Kent that include the Hoo Peninsula, the Medway estuary, the Swale, the Isle of Sheppey and the North Downs, but our outdoor meetings have ranged from anywhere in Kent to, via coach trips, Slimbridge in the west, Titchfield Haven in the south, Fingringhoe Wick in the east and Titchwell Marsh in the north. Sometimes even France.<br></span></p>
    <p class="Normal-P"><span class="Normal-C">Our indoor meetings cover a range of subjects, not just birds. We have had speakers, with accompanying slideshows, talking about everything from hedgehogs to fungi to seals and to trees - and covering subjects from all over the world.<br></span></p>
    <p class="Normal-P"><span class="Normal-C">The indoor meetings are held on the third Tuesday of every month, except August, at<a href="http://www.multimap.com/s/PFhGwRNm" target="_blank" style=""> Strood Library</a>, Bryant Road, Strood, ME2 3EP. The doors open at 7:00pm and the meetings start at 7:45pm and finish at 10:00pm. Refreshments are free and there is a raffle, the proceeds of which go to the RSPB. Admission prices are Â£2.50 for group members and Â£3.00 for non-members<br></span></p>
    <p class="Normal-P"><span class="Normal-C">At our outdoor meetings (which are free) and coach trips we welcome anybody, novice or expert, member or not. If you see something that you donât recognise there will be plenty of people willing to give a hand with identification, and not just with birds.<br></span></p>
    <p class="Normal-P"><span class="Normal-C">The group publishes a quarterly newsletter and a programme of indoor and outdoor meetings, both of which can be read on this website.</span><span class="Normal-C0"></span></p>
</div>

<h2 class='date-header'><span>Friday, February 12, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post'>
<a name='8937077571371785649'></a>
<h3 class='post-title'>
<a href='http://www.hawkowlsnest.com/2010/02/puerto-rico-non-avian-highlights.html'>Puerto Rico &#8211; Non-Avian Highlights</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
<div class='post-body'>
<p><p>Birds weren&#8217;t all that we saw in Puerto Rico. There were many butterflies seen flitting about that I didn&#8217;t get photographs of. These included Great Southern White, Cloudless Sulphur, and Florida Purplewing. I did get one decent butterfly photo of a Mallow Scrub-Hairstreak.</p><p align="center"><a href="http://lh3.ggpht.com/_2gstGUqAi0c/S3XFe-SyoJI/AAAAAAAABKc/Vv-tofmyEA4/s1600-h/mallow%20scrub-hairstreak%5B2%5D.jpg"><img title="mallow scrub-hairstreak" style="BORDER-RIGHT: 0px; BORDER-TOP: 0px; DISPLAY: inline; BORDER-LEFT: 0px; BORDER-BOTTOM: 0px" height="275" alt="mallow scrub-hairstreak" src="http://lh4.ggpht.com/_2gstGUqAi0c/S3XFgM0O_dI/AAAAAAAABKg/w1I_y18f5uo/mallow%20scrub-hairstreak_thumb.jpg?imgmax=800" width="412" border="0" /></a> </p><p align="center">Mallow Scrub-Hairstreak</p><p>We found two funky-looking moths. The first flew into the restaurant during dinner in Maricao. It&#8217;s about the size of a monarch.</p><p align="center"><a href="http://lh5.ggpht.com/_2gstGUqAi0c/S3XFgnHZb7I/AAAAAAAABKk/smgId1kQhGs/s1600-h/Funky%20Moth1%5B2%5D.jpg"><img title="Funky Moth1" style="BORDER-RIGHT: 0px; BORDER-TOP: 0px; DISPLAY: block; FLOAT: none; MARGIN-LEFT: auto; BORDER-LEFT: 0px; MARGIN-RIGHT: auto; BORDER-BOTTOM: 0px" height="276" alt="Funky Moth1" src="http://lh4.ggpht.com/_2gstGUqAi0c/S3XFhA8etmI/AAAAAAAABKo/5ssRGAdv_Ng/Funky%20Moth1_thumb.jpg?imgmax=800" width="412" border="0" /></a></p><p></p><p>The second was found at Casa Cubuy in El Yunque. This one was about 3&#8221; long.</p><p align="center"><a href="http://lh3.ggpht.com/_2gstGUqAi0c/S3XFhyDC5RI/AAAAAAAABKs/k8a46URhBzo/s1600-h/funky%20moth2%5B2%5D.jpg"><img title="funky moth2" style="BORDER-RIGHT: 0px; BORDER-TOP: 0px; DISPLAY: block; FLOAT: none; MARGIN-LEFT: auto; BORDER-LEFT: 0px; MARGIN-RIGHT: auto; BORDER-BOTTOM: 0px" height="276" alt="funky moth2" src="http://lh3.ggpht.com/_2gstGUqAi0c/S3XFiszfKNI/AAAAAAAABK0/A0dFUwAyhmk/funky%20moth2_thumb.jpg?imgmax=800" width="412" border="0" /></a></p><p></p><p>Lizards are plentiful in Puerto Rico. The most common are of the Anolis genus like the one below.</p><p align="center"><a href="http://lh3.ggpht.com/_2gstGUqAi0c/S3XFi6_a6TI/AAAAAAAABK4/4OcdwWioD0U/s1600-h/lizard2%5B2%5D.jpg"><img title="lizard2" style="BORDER-RIGHT: 0px; BORDER-TOP: 0px; DISPLAY: block; FLOAT: none; MARGIN-LEFT: auto; BORDER-LEFT: 0px; MARGIN-RIGHT: auto; BORDER-BOTTOM: 0px" height="276" alt="lizard2" src="http://lh5.ggpht.com/_2gstGUqAi0c/S3XFjnkh9AI/AAAAAAAABK8/NqwOLFKH8Bk/lizard2_thumb.jpg?imgmax=800" width="412" border="0" /></a></p><p></p><p>We also saw this interesting lizard in a tree. Maybe someone out there knows the ID. It&#8217;s probably another anole! (Update - might be <a href="http://www.wildherps.com/species/A.cristatellus.html">Puerto Rican Crested Anole</a>)</p><p align="center"><a href="http://lh5.ggpht.com/_2gstGUqAi0c/S3XFkPpLS7I/AAAAAAAABLA/tYdRXyzcee0/s1600-h/lizard%5B2%5D.jpg"><img title="lizard" style="BORDER-RIGHT: 0px; BORDER-TOP: 0px; DISPLAY: block; FLOAT: none; MARGIN-LEFT: auto; BORDER-LEFT: 0px; MARGIN-RIGHT: auto; BORDER-BOTTOM: 0px" height="276" alt="lizard" src="http://lh4.ggpht.com/_2gstGUqAi0c/S3XFkif0VUI/AAAAAAAABLE/dHzdfW_ysDE/lizard_thumb.jpg?imgmax=800" width="412" border="0" /></a></p><p></p><p>This concludes all of the Puerto Rico posts I had planned. It was a fantastic trip!</p></p>
<div style='clear: both;'></div>

<h2 class='date-header'><span>Tuesday, February 09, 2010</span></h2>

        <div class="date-posts">
      
<div class='post-outer'>
<div class='post'>
<a name='1649320717731108840'></a>
<h3 class='post-title'>
<a href='http://www.hawkowlsnest.com/2010/02/puerto-rico-bird-list.html'>Puerto Rico Bird List</a>
</h3>
<div class='post-header-line-1'></div>
<div class='post-body'>
<p>Here is the complete list of species seen. Asterisk denotes an endemic.<div><br /></div><div><div><br /></div><div>DUCKS, GEESE, AND WATERFOWL</div><div>West Indian Whistling-Duck</div><div>Blue-winged Teal</div><div>White-cheeked Pintail</div><div>GREBES</div><div>Least Grebe</div><div>BOOBIES AND GANNETS</div><div>Brown Booby</div><div>PELICANS</div><div>Brown Pelican</div><div>FRIGATEBIRDS</div><div>Magnificent Frigatebird</div><div>HERONS, EGRETS, AND BITTERNS</div><div>Great Blue Heron</div><div>Great Egret</div><div>Snowy Egret</div><div>Little Blue Heron</div><div>Cattle Egret</div><div>Green Heron</div><div>Black-crowned Night-Heron</div><div>IBISES AND SPOONBILLS</div><div>Glossy Ibis</div><div>NEW WORLD VULTURES</div><div>Turkey Vulture</div><div>OSPREY</div><div>Osprey</div><div>HAWKS, EAGLES, AND KITES</div><div>Broad-winged Hawk</div><div>Red-tailed Hawk</div><div>FALCONS AND CARACARAS</div><div>Peregrine Falcon</div><div>RAILS, GALLINULES, AND COOTS</div><div>Sora</div><div>Purple Gallinule</div><div>Common Moorhen</div><div>American Coot</div><div>Caribbean Coot</div><div>SANDPIPERS AND ALLIES</div><div>Spotted Sandpiper</div><div>Greater Yellowlegs</div><div>Lesser Yellowlegs</div><div>Ruddy Turnstone</div><div>Dunlin</div><div>TERNS</div><div>Royal Tern</div><div>PIGEONS AND DOVES</div><div>Rock Pigeon</div><div>Scaly-naped Pigeon</div><div>Plain Pigeon</div><div>Eurasian Collared-Dove</div><div>White-winged Dove</div><div>Zenaida Dove</div><div>Common Ground-Dove</div><div>PARROTS</div><div>Monk Parakeet</div><div>CUCKOOS</div><div>Mangrove Cuckoo</div><div>Puerto Rican Lizard-Cuckoo*</div><div>Smooth-billed Ani</div><div>OWLS</div><div>Puerto Rican Screech-Owl (heard)*</div><div>NIGHTJARS AND ALLIES</div><div>Puerto Rican Nightjar (heard)*</div><div>HUMMINGBIRDS</div><div>Antillean Mango</div><div>Green Mango*</div><div>Green-throated Carib</div><div>Antillean Crested Hummingbird</div><div>Puerto Rican Emerald*</div><div>TODIES</div><div>Puerto Rican Tody*</div><div>KINGFISHERS</div><div>Belted Kingfisher</div><div>WOODPECKERS</div><div>Puerto Rican Woodpecker*</div><div>TYRANT FLYCATCHERS</div><div>Lesser Antillean Pewee</div><div>Puerto Rican Flycatcher*</div><div>Gray Kingbird</div><div>Loggerhead Kingbird</div><div>VIREOS</div><div>Puerto Rican Vireo*</div><div>SWALLOWS</div><div>Caribbean Martin</div><div>Barn Swallow</div><div>Cave Swallow</div><div>THRUSHES AND ALLIES</div><div>Red-legged Thrush</div><div>MOCKINGBIRDS AND THRASHERS</div><div>Northern Mockingbird</div><div>Pearly-eyed Thrasher</div><div>NEW WORLD WARBLERS</div><div>Northern Parula</div><div>Black-throated Blue Warbler</div><div>Adelaide's Warbler*</div><div>Prairie Warbler</div><div>Elfin-woods Warbler*</div><div>Black-and-white Warbler</div><div>BANANAQUIT</div><div>Bananaquit</div><div>TANAGERS AND ALLIES</div><div>Puerto Rican Tanager*</div><div>Puerto Rican Spindalis*</div><div>BUNTINGS,SPARROWS,SEEDEATERS,ALLIES </div><div>Yellow-faced Grassquit</div><div>Black-faced Grassquit</div><div>Puerto Rican Bullfinch*</div><div>TROUPIALS AND ALLIES</div><div>Yellow-shouldered Blackbird*</div><div>Greater Antillean Grackle</div><div>Shiny Cowbird</div><div>Greater Antillean Oriole</div><div>Venezuelan Troupial</div><div>SISKINS, CROSSBILLS, AND ALLIES</div><div>Antillean Euphonia</div><div>OLD WORLD SPARROWS</div><div>House Sparrow</div><div>WEAVERS AND ALLIES</div><div>Orange Bishop</div><div>WAXBILLS AND ALLIES</div><div>Orange-cheeked Waxbill</div><div>Bronze Mannikin</div><div>Nutmeg Mannikin</div><div>-------- STATISTICS --------</div><div>Species seen - 86</div><div><br /></div></div></p>
